Judge Delegation: The Agency Analogy and Qualification Conditions
Explaining the analogy between judicial delegation and agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a judge cannot delegate without permission by identifying the analogy to wakalah and stating the conditions under which a deputy's judgment is valid.

Core Idea
A judge's unauthorized deputy's judgment is valid only if the deputy is qualified and the judge later validates it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes a foundational rule in judicial delegation: a judge who has been delegated authority cannot act beyond its scope, just as an agent (wakil) cannot appoint another agent without the principal's permission. Using the analogy to agency (wakalah), the text explains that if a judge appoints a deputy without authorization, the deputy's judgment can be validated if the judge subsequently ratifies it, provided the deputy is legally qualified for judgeship. Disqualifying factors include being a slave, having received the hadd punishment for slander, or being a non-Muslim. The ruling is derived from the general Hanafi principle that delegation of authority is akin to agency in requiring consent for sub-delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
The text compares judicial delegation to wakalah (agency). State the basic agency rule: an agent cannot appoint a sub-agent without the principal's permission. This is the same for a judge: he cannot appoint a deputy without the permission of the delegating authority.
The text says: 'like a wakeel does not appoint without the permission of the principal.'
2
Extract the condition for validity of the deputy's judgment
According to the text, if a judge appoints a deputy without permission and the deputy gives a judgment, the judgment becomes valid when the judge validates it, but only if the deputy is qualified (ahl) for judgeship. List the disqualifying factors mentioned: being a slave, having received the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of adultery), or being a non-Muslim.
The text states: 'if he appointed a deputy without permission... it is valid provided the deputy was qualified for judgeship. If he was a slave... or a disbeliever, it is not valid.'
3
Note the exception for presence
The text adds that even if the deputy is qualified, if he judges in the presence of the original judge, the judgment is invalid. This is because the objective of the deputy's appointment is to have the judge's own opinion present; if the judge is present, the deputy's judgment is redundant.
The text says: 'Likewise if he judged in the presence of the judge, as in agency, because the objective is the presence of his opinion.'

Source Text

كتاب الحوالة باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وغيره أن التقليد به فلا يتصرف في غير ما فوض إليه كالوكيل لا يوكل بدون إذن الموكل. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان بعذر أو لا كما في العناية، فلو استخلف بلا إذن فحكم الخليفة فأجازه القاضي جاز حيث كان الخليفة أهلاً للقضاء، فإن كان رقيقاً أو محدوداً في قذف أو كافراً لم يجز، وكذا إذا قضى بحضرة القاضي كما في الوكالة لأن المقصود حضور رأيه. وفي آخر جامع الفصولين : القاضي لو قضى في كل أسبوع يومين بأن كان له ولاية القضاء في يومين من كل أسبوع لا غير فقضى في الأيام التي لم تكن له ولاية القضاء، فإذا جاءت نوبته أجاز ما قضى جازت ا هـ . فدخل الفضولي في القضاء وهو أعم من القاضي، وظاهر كلامهم إجازة قضاء الفضولي لا تتوقف على كون الفضولي خليفة من قاض ليس له ولاية الاستخلاف، بل لو قضى فضولي بلا استخلاف أصلاً فأجازه القاضي جاز. ثم اعلم أن قولهم كما في الوكالة معناه الوكالة بالبيع والنكاح ونحوهما، أما الوكيل بالطلاق والعتاق إذا أجاز أو حضر لم يصح لأن المقصود عبارته كما في المنية وشمل التفويض إليه ما إذا كان صريحاً بأن قال له ول من شئت أو دلالة كجعلتك قاضي القضاة، والدلالة هنا أقوى لأن في الصريح المذكور يملك الاستخلاف لا العزل وفي الدلالة يملكهما كقوله «ول من شئت» و «استبدل من شئت فإن قاضي القضاة هو الذي يتصرف فيهم مطلقاً تقليداً وعزلاً. وإذا قال له ول من شئت واستخلف كان نائباً عن الإمام في التولية فلا يملك عزله كالوكيل إذا وكل بإذن ولا ينعزل بموته وينعزلان بموت الموكل بخلاف الوصي حيث يملك الإيصاء إلى غيره، ويملك التوكيل والعزل في حياته لرضى الموصي بذلك دلالة لعجزه بخلاف الإمام والموكل، وبخلاف المستعير فإن له الإعارة بشرطه لأنه لما ملك المنفعة ملك تمليكها. وفي الملتقط : القاضي إذا استخلف خليفة فقضى للقاضي لا يجوز، والطريق فيه أن فاستخلف غيره جاز فهذا القاضي الثاني يصير قاضياً من جهة الخليفة لا من جهة القاضي الأوّل حتى لو أراد القاضي الأوّل أن يعزل الثاني لم يكن له ذلك إلا إذا قال الخليفة للأوّل تستبدل من شئت اهـ قوله : (فدخل الفضولي في القضاء المصرح به أن القضاء عقد من العقود وأن كل عقد لا مجيز له حال صدوره من الفضولي لا يتوقف فكذا يجب أن يكون قضاء الفضولي إن كان له مجيز حال صدوره يتوقف ومالا فلا كدار الحرب حيث لا سلطان ولا قاضي ولو قضى بعد منعه ينبغي أن يكون موقوفاً، وفتوى من أفتى بعدم الصحة محمولة على عدم النفاذ حتى يجاز، وبعدم صحة إجازته محمول على الإجازة الإجمالية فتأمل والله تعالى الموفق
Book of Hawala, Chapter of the Judge's Letter to Another Judge etc. That delegation to him, so he does not act in other than what was entrusted to him, like a wakeel does not appoint without the permission of the principal. It is unrestricted so it includes whether with an excuse or not, as in al-‘Inayah. So if he appointed a deputy without permission, and the deputy gave a judgment, then the judge validated it, it is valid provided the deputy was qualified for judgeship. If he was a slave, or one who had received the hadd punishment for slander, or a disbeliever, it is not valid. Likewise if he judged in the presence of the judge, as in agency, because the objective is the presence of his opinion. At the end of Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: If a judge judged two days a week, having jurisdiction only those two days, and he judged on days when he had no jurisdiction, then when his turn came and he validated what he judged, it is valid. So the unauthorized person (fuduli) enters into judgeship, and it is more general than a judge. The apparent import of their statements is that the validation of a fuduli's judgment does not depend on the fuduli being a deputy of a judge who does not have authority to appoint a deputy. Rather, even if a fuduli judged with no appointment at all, and the judge validated it, it is valid. Then know that their statement 'as in agency' means agency for sale, marriage, and the like. As for a wakeel for divorce or manumission, if he validated or was present, it is not valid because the objective is his expression, as in al-Munyah. And delegation to him includes whether explicit, by saying to him 'appoint whomever you wish', or implicit, such as making him chief judge. The implicit is stronger here because in the explicit mentioned, he possesses the power to appoint a deputy but not to dismiss, while in the implicit he possesses both, as when one says 'appoint whomever you wish' and 'replace whomever you wish', for a chief judge is one who has full authority over them in appointment and dismissal. If he said to him 'appoint whomever you wish' and he appointed a deputy, he is a representative of the imam in the appointment, so he cannot dismiss him, like a wakeel who has been given permission to appoint, and he is not dismissed by the death of the principal, but both are dismissed by the death of the principal, contrary to a wasi, who can delegate to another, and he can appoint an agent and dismiss him during his lifetime because of the testator's implied consent due to his incapacity, contrary to the imam and the principal, and contrary to a borrower, who may lend with its condition because when he owns the usufruct he owns the right to transfer it. In al-Multaqat: If a judge appointed a deputy and the deputy judged for the judge, it is not valid. The way in that is that if he appointed another deputy, it is valid; this second judge becomes a judge from the perspective of the deputy, not from the perspective of the first judge, so much so that if the first judge wanted to dismiss the second, he cannot, unless the deputy says to the first 'replace whomever you wish' end quote. His statement: 'So the fuduli enters into judgeship' indicates that judgeship is a contract among contracts, and every contract that has no validator at the time of its occurrence from a fuduli is not suspended, and likewise must be the case for a fuduli's judgment. If it has a validator at the time of occurrence, it is suspended, otherwise not, like dar al-harb where there is no sultan and no judge. If he judged after being prevented, it should be suspended. The fatwa of one who gives a fatwa of invalidity is understood to mean non-execution until validated, and the statement of the invalidity of its validation is understood to mean summary validation. So ponder, and Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the rules of a judge delegating his authority to someone else (khalifah) and the validation of a judgment made by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The text opens by stating that a judge who is delegated (muqallad) cannot act outside the scope of his delegation, similar to an agent (wakil) who cannot appoint another agent without the principal's permission, as firmly established in Hanafi law. This is expanded to include both excuses and no excuse, as mentioned in al-‘Inayah. The key point is that if a judge appoints a deputy without permission and later validates the judgment, it is valid only if the deputy is qualified (ahl) for judgeship. If the deputy is a slave, or has been subjected to the hadd punishment for qadhf, or is a non-Muslim, the judgment is invalid. This principle is extended to the case where the deputy judges in the presence of the original judge, mirroring agency law. The text then cites Jami‘ al-Fusulayn regarding a judge who has jurisdiction only two days a week: if he judges on other days and later validates the judgment during his appointed time, it becomes valid. This leads to the concept of the fuduli (unauthorized person) in judgeship: the apparent position of the Hanafi masters is that if a person with no authority whatsoever gives a judgment, and the proper judge later validates it, it is valid. This is based on the general principle that every contract that has a potential validator at the time of occurrence is suspended pending validation; otherwise it is not suspended. The text distinguishes between explicit and implicit delegation: explicit delegation (like 'appoint whomever you wish') grants only appointment power, not dismissal; implicit delegation (like making someone chief judge) grants both, because the chief judge has full authority. The passage also notes that if a judge gives his deputy permission to appoint another, the second deputy is a representative of the first deputy, not the original judge, so the original judge cannot dismiss him directly. The author concludes by advising reflection on the fatwa that fuduli's judgment is invalid, explaining it as meaning non-execution, not absolute invalidity.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid): Delegation of judicial authority from a higher authority (imam or superior judge) to a judge
خليفة (khalifah): Deputy judge appointed by a judge to act in his place
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized person who acts without any appointment or authority
إجازة (ijazah): Validation of a prior unauthorized act (like a judgment) by the person who has authority
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; the analogy used for delegation in judicial matters

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of a judge validating a deputy's judgment when the deputy was appointed without permission?
A) The judgment remains invalid regardless of the deputy's qualification.
B) The judgment becomes valid only if the deputy is not a slave, not hadd-punished for qadhf, and not a non-Muslim.
C) The judgment becomes valid only if the deputy judged in the presence of the judge.
D) The judgment becomes valid only if the original judge had an excuse.

Key Takeaway
A judge's unauthorized deputy's judgment is validated by the judge's subsequent ratification, provided the deputy is legally qualified (free, Muslim, not hadd-punished for qadhf) and did not judge in the judge's presence.

Validation of a Fuduli's Judgment
Explaining the validation of judgments by an unauthorized person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the validity of a fuduli's (unauthorized person's) judgment and identify the condition for its validation.

Core Idea
A fuduli's judgment can be validated by the proper judge; it is not automatically void.

This part of the passage extends the principle of validation (ijazah) from the case of an unauthorized deputy to a complete stranger (fuduli) who gives a judgment without any appointment. Drawing on the principle that judgeship is a contract (عقد), the Hanafi school holds that if a person with no authority issues a judgment, and the proper judge later ratifies it, it becomes valid. This is analogous to the rule for contracts (like sale) made by a fuduli: they are suspended pending ratification. The text also addresses a special case from Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: a judge with limited jurisdiction (only two days a week) can validate judgments he gave on other days when his turn comes. This shows the flexibility of the Hanafi system in upholding justice.

Guided Path
1
Define fuduli in the judicial context
A fuduli is a person who acts without any authority or appointment. In judgeship, this is someone who gives a judgment without being a judge or a deputy.
The text says: 'the unauthorized person (fuduli) enters into judgeship, and it is more general than a judge.'
2
Identify the Hanafi rule on validation
The text states that the apparent position of the Hanafi masters is that the validation (ijazah) of a fuduli's judgment does not require the fuduli to be a deputy of a judge who lacks authority to appoint. Even if he judges with no appointment at all, the judge's validation makes it valid.
The text says: 'the apparent import of their statements is that the validation of a fuduli's judgment does not depend on the fuduli being a deputy... Rather, even if a fuduli judged with no appointment at all, and the judge validated it, it is valid.'
3
Examine the case of limited jurisdiction
The text cites Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: if a judge has jurisdiction only on two days a week and judges on other days, his validation of those judgments when his turn comes makes them valid. This illustrates that even a judge acting beyond his scope can validate his own prior unauthorized judgments.
The text says: 'If a judge judged two days a week... and he judged on days when he had no jurisdiction, then when his turn came and he validated what he judged, it is valid.'
4
Note the principle underlying the ruling
The text generalizes: 'judgeship is a contract among contracts, and every contract that has no validator at the time of its occurrence from a fuduli is not suspended; otherwise it is suspended.' This means that if there is a potential validator at the time of the judgment, it is suspended pending ratification; if not (e.g., in dar al-harb with no authority), it is not suspended but may still be ratified later.
The text says: 'the judgment that judgeship is a contract... every contract that has no validator at the time of occurrence from a fuduli is not suspended, otherwise it is suspended.'

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the rules of a judge delegating his authority to someone else (khalifah) and the validation of a judgment made by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The text opens by stating that a judge who is delegated (muqallad) cannot act outside the scope of his delegation, similar to an agent (wakil) who cannot appoint another agent without the principal's permission, as firmly established in Hanafi law. This is expanded to include both excuses and no excuse, as mentioned in al-‘Inayah. The key point is that if a judge appoints a deputy without permission and later validates the judgment, it is valid only if the deputy is qualified (ahl) for judgeship. If the deputy is a slave, or has been subjected to the hadd punishment for qadhf, or is a non-Muslim, the judgment is invalid. This principle is extended to the case where the deputy judges in the presence of the original judge, mirroring agency law. The text then cites Jami‘ al-Fusulayn regarding a judge who has jurisdiction only two days a week: if he judges on other days and later validates the judgment during his appointed time, it becomes valid. This leads to the concept of the fuduli (unauthorized person) in judgeship: the apparent position of the Hanafi masters is that if a person with no authority whatsoever gives a judgment, and the proper judge later validates it, it is valid. This is based on the general principle that every contract that has a potential validator at the time of occurrence is suspended pending validation; otherwise it is not suspended. The text distinguishes between explicit and implicit delegation: explicit delegation (like 'appoint whomever you wish') grants only appointment power, not dismissal; implicit delegation (like making someone chief judge) grants both, because the chief judge has full authority. The passage also notes that if a judge gives his deputy permission to appoint another, the second deputy is a representative of the first deputy, not the original judge, so the original judge cannot dismiss him directly. The author concludes by advising reflection on the fatwa that fuduli's judgment is invalid, explaining it as meaning non-execution, not absolute invalidity.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized person who acts without any appointment or authority, whether in contracts or judgments
إجازة (ijazah): Validation of a prior unauthorized act by the person who has authority
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended pending ratification; a legal act that is not final until validated

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a fuduli's judgment if there is no potential validator at the time of its occurrence (e.g., in dar al-harb)?
A) It is automatically void.
B) It is not suspended (mawquf) but can be validated later.
C) It is suspended and must be ratified.
D) It is valid immediately.

Key Takeaway
A fuduli's judgment is not automatically void; it can be validated by the proper judge, based on the principle that judgeship is a contract and subject to the rules of ijazah.

Explicit vs Implicit Delegation and the Deputy's Appointment of Another
Distinguishing explicit and implicit delegation and the chain of deputy appointments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit and implicit judicial delegation in Hanafi law and state the authority of a deputy appointed by another deputy.

Core Idea
Implicit delegation by making someone chief judge grants broader authority than explicit delegation, and a deputy's deputy is the deputy's own judge, not the original judge's.

This section of the passage delves into the nuances of how a judge can be delegated authority: either explicitly (by saying 'appoint whomever you wish') or implicitly (by being appointed chief judge). The implicit form is stronger because it grants both the power to appoint deputies and the power to dismiss them, whereas the explicit form only grants the power to appoint. The text also explains the consequence when a deputy (appointed with explicit permission) himself appoints another deputy: the second deputy is a representative of the imam via the first deputy, so the original judge cannot dismiss him. This is contrasted with the case of a wasi (executor) and a borrower. The passage also clarifies a report from al-Multaqat: if a judge appoints a deputy and that deputy judges for the judge (i.e., in the judge's own court?), it is invalid; but if the deputy appoints another, that second judge becomes the deputy's judge.

Guided Path
1
Define explicit and implicit delegation
Explicit delegation is when the Imam says to the judge: 'appoint whomever you wish' (ول من شئت). Implicit delegation is when the Imam says: 'I make you chief judge' (جعلتك قاضي القضاة).
The text says: 'delegation to him includes whether explicit... or implicit.'
2
Identify the scope of authority in each case
In explicit delegation (saying 'appoint whomever you wish'), the judge has the power to appoint deputies but cannot dismiss them. In implicit delegation (making him chief judge), the judge has both powers: appointment and dismissal.
The text says: 'in the explicit mentioned, he possesses the power to appoint a deputy but not to dismiss, while in the implicit he possesses both.'
3
Analyze the consequence of a deputy appointed by a deputy
If a judge (who has explicit delegation) appoints a deputy, and that deputy appoints another deputy, the second deputy is a representative of the Imam through the first deputy. Therefore, the original judge cannot dismiss the second deputy directly; only the first deputy can, unless the first deputy gave the original judge permission.
The text says: 'If he said to him 'appoint whomever you wish' and he appointed a deputy, he is a representative of the Imam in the appointment, so he cannot dismiss him.'
4
Examine the report from al-Multaqat
Al-Multaqat states: If a judge appoints a deputy and that deputy judges for the judge (i.e., in the judge's name or case?), it is invalid. The solution (الطريق) is that if the deputy appoints another deputy, that second deputy is valid and becomes a judge from the perspective of the first deputy, not the original judge. So the original judge cannot dismiss him.
The text says: 'In al-Multaqat: If a judge appointed a deputy and the deputy judged for the judge, it is not valid. The way is that if he appointed another, it is valid; this second judge becomes a judge from the perspective of the deputy.'

It is unrestricted so it includes whether with an excuse or not, as in al-‘Inayah. So if he appointed a deputy without permission, and the deputy gave a judgment, then the judge validated it, it is valid provided the deputy was qualified for judgeship. If he was a slave, or one who had received the hadd punishment for slander, or a disbeliever, it is not valid. Likewise if he judged in the presence of the judge, as in agency, because the objective is the presence of his opinion. At the end of Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: If a judge judged two days a week, having jurisdiction only those two days, and he judged on days when he had no jurisdiction, then when his turn came and he validated what he judged, it is valid. So the unauthorized person (fuduli) enters into judgeship, and it is more general than a judge. The apparent import of their statements is that the validation of a fuduli's judgment does not depend on the fuduli being a deputy of a judge who does not have authority to appoint a deputy. Rather, even if a fuduli judged with no appointment at all, and the judge validated it, it is valid. Then know that their statement 'as in agency' means agency for sale, marriage, and the like. As for a wakeel for divorce or manumission, if he validated or was present, it is not valid because the objective is his expression, as in al-Munyah. And delegation to him includes whether explicit, by saying to him 'appoint whomever you wish', or implicit, such as making him chief judge. The implicit is stronger here because in the explicit mentioned, he possesses the power to appoint a deputy but not to dismiss, while in the implicit he possesses both, as when one says 'appoint whomever you wish' and 'replace whomever you wish', for a chief judge is one who has full authority over them in appointment and dismissal. If he said to him 'appoint whomever you wish' and he appointed a deputy, he is a representative of the imam in the appointment, so he cannot dismiss him, like a wakeel who has been given permission to appoint, and he is not dismissed by the death of the principal, but both are dismissed by the death of the principal, contrary to a wasi, who can delegate to another, and he can appoint an agent and dismiss him during his lifetime because of the testator's implied consent due to his incapacity, contrary to the imam and the principal, and contrary to a borrower, who may lend with its condition because when he owns the usufruct he owns the right to transfer it. In al-Multaqat: If a judge appointed a deputy and the deputy judged for the judge, it is not valid. The way in that is that if he appointed another deputy, it is valid; this second judge becomes a judge from the perspective of the deputy, not from the perspective of the first judge, so much so that if the first judge wanted to dismiss the second, he cannot, unless the deputy says to the first 'replace whomever you wish' end quote. His statement: 'So the fuduli enters into judgeship' indicates that judgeship is a contract among contracts, and every contract that has no validator at the time of its occurrence from a fuduli is not suspended, and likewise must be the case for a fuduli's judgment. If it has a validator at the time of occurrence, it is suspended, otherwise not, like dar al-harb where there is no sultan and no judge. If he judged after being prevented, it should be suspended. The fatwa of one who gives a fatwa of invalidity is understood to mean non-execution until validated, and the statement of the invalidity of its validation is understood to mean summary validation. So ponder, and Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the rules of a judge delegating his authority to someone else (khalifah) and the validation of a judgment made by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The text opens by stating that a judge who is delegated (muqallad) cannot act outside the scope of his delegation, similar to an agent (wakil) who cannot appoint another agent without the principal's permission, as firmly established in Hanafi law. This is expanded to include both excuses and no excuse, as mentioned in al-‘Inayah. The key point is that if a judge appoints a deputy without permission and later validates the judgment, it is valid only if the deputy is qualified (ahl) for judgeship. If the deputy is a slave, or has been subjected to the hadd punishment for qadhf, or is a non-Muslim, the judgment is invalid. This principle is extended to the case where the deputy judges in the presence of the original judge, mirroring agency law. The text then cites Jami‘ al-Fusulayn regarding a judge who has jurisdiction only two days a week: if he judges on other days and later validates the judgment during his appointed time, it becomes valid. This leads to the concept of the fuduli (unauthorized person) in judgeship: the apparent position of the Hanafi masters is that if a person with no authority whatsoever gives a judgment, and the proper judge later validates it, it is valid. This is based on the general principle that every contract that has a potential validator at the time of occurrence is suspended pending validation; otherwise it is not suspended. The text distinguishes between explicit and implicit delegation: explicit delegation (like 'appoint whomever you wish') grants only appointment power, not dismissal; implicit delegation (like making someone chief judge) grants both, because the chief judge has full authority. The passage also notes that if a judge gives his deputy permission to appoint another, the second deputy is a representative of the first deputy, not the original judge, so the original judge cannot dismiss him directly. The author concludes by advising reflection on the fatwa that fuduli's judgment is invalid, explaining it as meaning non-execution, not absolute invalidity.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid): Delegation of judicial authority, but in this context specifically the act of appointing a judge
استخلاف (istikhlaf): Appointment of a deputy judge (khalifah) by a judge
قاضي القضاة (qadi al-qudat): Chief judge, who has full authority over other judges including appointment and dismissal
وصي (wasi): Executor of a will, who has broad discretion to delegate due to the testator's implied trust

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what power does a judge gain when he is appointed with the explicit words 'appoint whomever you wish'?
A) Both the power to appoint and dismiss deputies.
B) Only the power to appoint deputies, not to dismiss them.
C) No power to appoint or dismiss; it is only advisory.
D) Only the power to dismiss deputies, not to appoint them.

Key Takeaway
The scope of a judge's power to delegate is determined by the wording of the appointment: explicit ('appoint whomever') grants only appointment, while implicit (chief judge) grants appointment and dismissal.

Can a Deputy Judge for the Original Judge? Intra-Madhhab Dispute on Judicial Bias
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of deputy's judgment for original judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on a deputy judging for the original judge and explain the legal reasoning behind each opinion, including the role of tuhmah (suspicion of bias).

Core Idea
A deputy may not judge for the original judge due to suspicion of partiality—his action is like the judge's own.

This passage from al-Ramli's hashya on al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a delicate question: can a deputy judge decide a case where the original judge (or his family) is a party? The Hanafi school is internally divided, with classical texts offering opposing rulings. The core issue turns on whether the deputy is considered a representative of the judge or of the public at large. Understanding this dispute is essential for grasping judicial ethics and the concept of tuhmah (suspicion) in Islamic court procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The passage addresses whether a deputy judge's judgment is valid when it involves the original judge or his son. Extract the exact scenario from the text.
The scenario arises when the original judge is a party or has a family member as a party.
2
Extract the Two Opinions
From the sources cited: al-Sirajiyyah allows it; al-Multaqat disallows it. Note that al-Bazzaziyyah supports al-Sirajiyyah initially, but later al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir sides with al-Multaqat.
Al-Ramli notes that he has not seen a decisive preference between the two.
3
Analyze the Reasoning
For allowance: deputy represents the sultan or the public, not the judge personally, so no conflict. For disallowance: deputy is like the judge; judging for the judge is like judging for oneself, creating tuhmah (suspicion of bias).
The difference hinges on the nature of the judge's representation: agent of the public vs. agent of the ruler.
4
Apply the Later Maxim
Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir states that in our time, deputies are considered representatives of the judge in every respect. This strengthens the disallowance view because the deputy's act becomes the judge's act.
This maxim is from the later Hanafi tradition (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
5
Identify the Preferred View
Al-Ramli suggests that the view from al-Multaqat (invalid) should be preferred due to tuhmah, but he concedes he has not found a clear tarjih in the sources. The issue remains unresolved within the school.
In practice, caution would require avoiding such a situation.

His statement: 'And the apparent import of their statements etc.' al-Ramli said: Rather it is included in their statement 'whenever agency is valid, if the fuduli undertakes it, it is suspended' and in their statement 'every contract that occurs and has a validator at the time of occurrence is contracted suspended upon his validation'. Judgeship is one of the shar‘i contracts, agency in it is valid with its condition; reflect. The discussion about this has preceded in the sale of the fuduli. His statement: 'If the judge judged for the imam who delegated judgeship to him, or for the imam's son, it is valid, end quote. In al-Sirajiyyah: If a judge has a case involving himself or his son, he delegates another qualified person, they litigate before him and he judges for him or his son; it is valid. If the judge judged for the imam who delegated judgeship to him, or for the imam's son, it is valid, end quote. In al-Bazzaziyyah, it is as in al-Sirajiyyah. In al-Khulasah: If the deputy gave permission to the judge to appoint a deputy, and he appointed a man and gave him permission to appoint a deputy, it is valid for him to appoint a deputy, and then another, end quote. In it: If they want to establish the deputy's judgment before the original judge, it is like establishing the judgment of another judge before this judge. In Adab al-Qadi by al-Sadr al-Shahid: The deputy judges by what was testified before the original judge, and likewise the original judge judges by what was testified before the deputy, end quote. In al-Bazzaziyyah: Khul‘ took place between the spouses before the judge twice; his deputy said: 'It took place before me another time' and the husband denied; the imam judge said: 'The judge cannot rule by the severe prohibition based on the deputy's statement; but the deputy can rule by the judge's statement if he informs him', end quote. Then he said in a type regarding execution: The deputy judges by what was testified before the original judge, and likewise the judge judges by what was testified before the deputy. The judge ordered the deputy concerning the case and the testimony, and he writes the acknowledgment but does not finalize the ruling; he does what he was ordered. The judge hears but he cannot judge. The judge cannot judge based on his deputy's report of witness testimony before him because he is not a judge. Likewise if a man informs him of his acknowledgment, unless he himself witnesses with another. And the responses of our imams in Khwarazm concurred that the testimony of a forced judge (musakhkhar) and the fabricated testimony of agents at his door are not accepted, unlike their deputies, except just people. And I saw in the regions of Khwarazm and there a group of those to whom judgeship was delegated, and likewise in some regions of Dasht, judgeship is not valid by their testimony, so how is their judgment? I was asked about the testimony of some of them that it is accepted; I transmitted: yes, it is accepted with two just witnesses. And all of that is from the laxity of the Dasht region regarding the Shari'ah. And I saw among the wonders that one of its amirs who claimed that no one like him had passed, delegated judgeship of a city to an ignorant youth who did not know Quran nor writing, so that he judges by four madhhabs. I said to him about it, and he said: 'I know better the maslahah, and Allah knows the corrupt from the righteous.' end quote. So the conclusion is that if the judge appoints the deputy to judgeship, he acts by what he was delegated. His statement: 'who delegated him' al-Ramli said: the reason is that the judge is a representative of the general public, as in al-Muhit. And if so, then his action is not attributed to him. And whoever said that the judge is a representative of the sultan, perhaps his reason is that the only path is in him, since the ruler from the imam is in the position of the appointed judge, there is no path to tahkim, so that is allowed to open the door of judgeship for him. It will come that the ruler from the imam is in the position of the judge. I have not seen anyone who has clarified that. Ponder. His statement: 'In al-Bazzaziyyah as in al-Sirajiyyah' al-Ramli said: So it is established that in the issue there are two opinions. The basis of what is in al-Sirajiyyah is that the deputy is not a representative of him, but rather is a representative of the sultan or the general public, so the relationship is cut off. But in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, they are deputies of the judge in our time in every respect, and accordingly, what is in al-Multaqat should be preferred because of the suspicion in his judging for him, since the deputy's action is like his action, so it is as if he judged for himself. I have not seen anyone prefer one of the two opinions, and Allah knows best. His statement: 'The judge ordered the deputy' meaning the deputy of the judge. His statement: 'end quote' meaning the speech of al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: 'But he mentioned'

Commentary
This passage contains the commentary (hashiya) of al-Ramli on the earlier text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, dealing with the validity of a deputy's judgment when it involves the original judge or his family, and the rules of testimony between the judge and his deputy. The passage opens with al-Ramli's note that the earlier point about the fuduli's judgment is included in the general principles of agency and contracts. Then it discusses the ruling from al-Sirajiyyah that if a judge has a case concerning himself or his son, he should delegate another qualified person; that person can validly judge in favor of the judge or his son. Similarly, if a judge judges for the imam who appointed him, it is valid. Al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Khulasah provide additional details: a deputy who is given permission to appoint further deputies can do so repeatedly. The passage then addresses the procedure for establishing a deputy's judgment before the original judge: it is like establishing another judge's judgment. The deputy can pass judgment based on testimony heard before the original judge, and vice versa. However, a crucial distinction arises: the judge cannot base a ruling on his deputy's bare report of witness testimony, nor on a man's report of an acknowledgment, unless the judge himself witnesses along with another. The text then shifts to a historical observation about corrupt practices in Khwarazm and Dasht, where unqualified individuals (including a youth ignorant of Quran and writing) were appointed judges, and the testimony of forced judges and fabricated agents is rejected. Al-Ramli then discusses the underlying legal principle: is the judge a representative of the general public (as in al-Muhit) or of the sultan? This affects whether the deputy's judgment is attributed to the judge. He notes two opinions: one (from al-Sirajiyyah) that the deputy is not the judge's representative but the sultan's or the public's, so the attribution is severed; another (from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir) that in our time they are deputies of the judge in every respect. Al-Ramli inclines towards preferring the view from al-Multaqat (that a deputy's judgment for the judge is invalid) because of the suspicion of partiality, but notes he has not seen a clear preference between the two. The passage ends with textual clarifications.

Key Terms
نائب (na'ib): Deputy judge, used interchangeably with khalifah
أصل (asl): The original judge who appointed the deputy
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony; the rules of accepting testimony before the deputy and the original judge
تهمة (tuhmah): Suspicion of partiality or bias; used as a ground to invalidate a judgment
حرمة غليظة (hurmah ghalizah): The severe prohibition (of remarriage after a triple divorce or similar)
مصلحة (maslahah): Public interest; used here to justify appointing unqualified judges by an ignorant ruler

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ramli's hashya, what is the preferred position regarding a deputy judging for the original judge?
A) It is always valid because the deputy is not the judge's representative
B) It is invalid because of suspicion of partiality, and this view is preferred
C) It is valid only if the original judge is the sultan
D) It is valid only if the deputy is qualified

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school is internally divided on whether a deputy can judge for the original judge, but later authorities lean toward invalidity to avoid tuhmah (suspicion of bias).

Validity of a Judge's Ruling Contrary to His Madhhab
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on judicial validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the validity conditions for a mujtahid and muqallid qadi's ruling contrary to his madhhab and apply the Hanafi tarjih to determine enforceability.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling contrary to his madhhab is not automatically void; its validity depends on his capacity and awareness.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates a delicate intra-Hanafi dispute: when a qadi issues a judgment that goes against his own school, does it stand or fall? The answer bifurcates along two axes: the capacity of the judge (mujtahid vs muqallid) and his state of mind (forgetful vs deliberate). Understanding this distinction is vital for any student of Hanafi fiqh, as it affects the enforceability of judicial decisions and the limits of a judge's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes of analysis
Every case in this passage is analyzed by: (a) Is the judge a mujtahid or muqallid? (b) Did he act forgetfully or deliberately? The ruling flips depending on these.
Mujtahid = capable of ijtihad; muqallid = follows a madhhab blindly.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa for each scenario
For a mujtahid: forgetful → valid; deliberate → two narrations (valid/invalid). For a muqallid: forgetful → valid; deliberate → invalid.
Abu Hanifa's two narrations for deliberate mujtahid reflect uncertainty.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf & Muhammad) for each scenario
For a mujtahid: both forgetful and deliberate invalid. For a muqallid: both invalid. They emphasize the appointment condition.
The Sahibayn prioritize the judge's mandate to rule by his madhhab.
4
Trace the later tarjih (preferred view) and the reasoning
Later authorities (e.g., Fath al-Qadir) favor the Sahibayn for mujtahids to prevent desire-driven rulings. For muqallids, some uphold validity if based on any recognized opinion, while others (al-Qunyah) say invalid. The author leans toward validity for muqallids based on the principle that a weak opinion is strengthened by the judge's ruling.
Tarjih is influenced by the presumption of base desire in deliberate opposition.
5
Note the reverse position of al-Bada'i' and its reconciliation
Al-Bada'i' reverses the condition: for non-mujtahid, deliberate invalid, forgetful valid per Abu Hanifa; for mujtahid, always valid because it is presumed ijtihad. The author finds this sound because a mujtahid's deliberate change is considered a valid ijtihad.
This shows the complexity of the ikhtilaf; al-Bada'i' offers a coherent alternative.

بخلاف مذهبه وقد نسيه وأما إذا قضى بخلاف مذهبه حال ذكر مذهبه لا يجوز حكمه بالإجماع اهـ. لكن في الشرنبلالية نقل عن شرح الجامع الكبير أنه العامد لا خلاف بين أصحابنا قال : والخلاف ثابت على الصحيح قوله : (والوجه في هذا الزمان (الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية نقل هذا في البرهان عن قال : وهذا صريح الحق الذي يعض عليه بالنواجذ قوله : (ثم اعلم أن عبارات المشايخ قد اختلفت الخ) قال في النهر بعدما مر آنفاً عن الفتح : ما تمسك به ما في الكمال ثم فصل مجتهد فيه ا هـ . وكذا في السراجية. وفي مآل الفتاوى : قضى بخلاف مذهبه وهو مختلف فيه قال أبو حنيفة ينفذ، وقال أبو يوسف لا ينفذ ا هـ. فقد تحرر أن القاضي المقلد إذا قضى باجتهاده فيصح ا هـ البزازية : إذا لم يكن القاضي مجتهداً وقضى بالفتوى الخ. وما في الفتح يجب أن يعوّل عليه في المذهب، وما في البزازية محمول على رواية عنهما إذ قصارى الأمر أن هذا منزل منزلة الناسي لمذهبه مثلاً لا السلطان المقلد - بكسر اللام - قوله : ( ومن العجب أن صاحب البدائع قيد الخلاف الخ) حاصله أن صاحب البدائع جعل الخلاف في نفاذ قضائه بخلاف مذهبه وعدم نفاذه في القاضي غير المجتهد عكس ما صرح به في الفتح بقوله : هذا كله في القاضي المجتهد. وقول الرملي ما تقدم من قوله وفي فتح القدير: لا يظهر منه أنه عكسه، وكذا قوله وما قيده به في فتح القدير فتأمل غير ظاهر ثم إن ما ذكره في البدائع وجيه فإن المجتهد إذا حكم بخلاف مذهبه عمداً كان ذلك رجوعاً عن مذهبه الأوّل لتغير اجتهاده ووجوب اتباع ما ظهر له ثانياً، وإن لم يعلم أنه خالفه عمداً يحمل عليه لا على أنه نسي مذهبه وحكم بخلافه إذ الأصل أنه فعل ذلك عمداً وهذا معنى قول البدائع . لأنه لا يصدق على النسيان الخ. اهـ .
Book of Hawala, Chapter of the Judge's Letter to Another Judge etc. In Khizanat al-Akmal: If he judges in an ijtihadi matter contrary to his own opinion while forgetful of his madhhab, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa. And regarding deliberate action, there are two narrations. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it does not take effect in both cases, and the tarjih differs. In al-Khaniyya: the more apparent of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa is the validity of his judgment, and upon it is the fatwa, etc. Likewise in al-Fatawa al-Sughra. And in al-Mi'raj citing al-Muhit: the fatwa is on their statement, and likewise in al-Hidaya. And in Fatawa Zahir al-Din, the sultan has the right to overturn it, etc. And in Fath al-Qadir: the fatwa differs, and the correct approach in this time is to give fatwa according to their statement, because the one who deliberately abandons his madhhab does so only for base desire, not for a good intention. As for the forgetful, the muqallid only appointed him to judge according to his madhhab, not another's. All this applies to the mujtahid judge. As for the muqallid, he was appointed to judge according to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, so he does not possess the capacity to oppose, and he is thus removed (from office). Then know that the expressions of the mashayikh have differed in this issue, namely regarding that ruling, etc. If the muqallid judges contrary to his own madhhab in agreement with the madhhab of a mujtahid: in al-Bazzaziyya citing Sharh al-Tahawi, if the judge is not a mujtahid and judges according to a fatwa, then it becomes clear that it is contrary to his madhhab, it is valid and no one else may overturn it according to Muhammad. And the second (Abu Yusuf) said: he also may not overturn it. So al-Imadi in al-Fusul. Then he said: the judge, if he judges in a matter of ijtihad while he holds a contrary view in some places, in some places it is not valid and in some it is valid, and he did not mention a difference in it; the correct view is that there is a difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. And in al-Muhit, it is mentioned that the narration differs in some cases regarding the validity of the judgment and in some cases regarding the permissibility of undertaking the judgment, etc. And he has a difference between them. And likewise mentioned in Umdat al-Fatawa: if the judge judges according to an abandoned opinion, it is permissible; likewise if he judges in a matter that has been raised to the second judge. Then this issue is different from the previous one, because the previous one is the condition that the first judge be knowledgeable of the difference so that his judgment is valid, and this is regarding the condition that he judges according to his own madhhab, not if he is forgetful and judges according to another's madhhab. His statement: (And regarding deliberate, two narrations) and it is as if he confined it in Mu'in al-Hukkam to the narration of invalidity, so he claimed consensus, where he said: as transmitted by al-Ramli when mentioning the judge Abu Bakr al-Razi, may Allah have mercy on him, the difference when he judges contrary to his madhhab and has forgotten it; as for when he judges contrary to his madhhab while remembering his madhhab, his judgment is not permissible by consensus. But in al-Sharnabaliyya, it is transmitted from Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir that regarding deliberate there is no difference among our associates; he said: the difference is established as correct. His statement: (And the correct approach in this time etc.) He said in al-Sharnabaliyya: this is transmitted in al-Burhan from him said: this is the clear truth that one should bite onto with molars. His statement: (Then know that the expressions of the mashayikh have differed etc.) He said in al-Nahr after what passed earlier from al-Fath: it is apparent that being knowledgeable of the difference applies only to the mujtahid judge. And in al-Qunyah: the muqallid judge, if he judges contrary to his madhhab, it is not valid. And he claimed in al-Bahr that the muqallid, if he judges according to another madhhab, or a weak narration, or a weak opinion, it is valid; and the strongest thing he held onto is what is in al-Kamal. Then he said: A section on ijtihadi matters, etc. Likewise in al-Sirajiyya. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: he judged contrary to his madhhab and it is disputed: Abu Hanifa said it is valid; Abu Yusuf said it is not valid, etc. So it is established that the muqallid judge, if he judges according to another madhhab, it is valid; likewise if he judges according to a weak narration or weak opinion, due to the general nature of their statement that the weak opinion is strengthened by the judgment of the judge. What was restricted by him in Fath al-Qadir, that this only applies to the mujtahid, is confirmed in some expressions. Therefore, he said in al-Qunyah: the muqallid judge, if he judges contrary to his madhhab, it is not valid, etc. This is contradicted by what his teacher Shaykh Umar Qari' al-Hidaya gave as a fatwa when asked about a waqf that had not been ruled upon, the founder retracted from it and dedicated it to another purpose, and a Hanafi judge ruled upon it; is the second valid or the first? He answered that the second is correct, even if the fatwa is contrary to the statement of Abu Hanifa, but it is supported by the ruling of the judge. And in Sharh Manzumah of Ibn Wahban, he has the scenario of the issue: if a judge rules in an incident with a ruling contrary to the madhhab of his muqallid (with fatha on the lam) meaning the imam he follows, and this is when the judge is a muqallid and is not among the people of ijtihad, like the Hanafi judges in our time for example, is his judgment valid or not? The answer is that if he is aware of his madhhab, it is not permissible; otherwise, it is permissible according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to them (the two). And it is strange that the author of al-Bada'i' restricted the difference in reverse of what is in Fath al-Qadir, saying: what is transmitted that if the judge judges contrary to his madhhab deliberately, it occurs as void; and if he is forgetful, according to him it is valid, according to them it is not valid; and this is when the judge is not among the people of ijtihad. But if he is among the people of ijtihad, then it befits that his judgment is valid by consensus, and no other judge may invalidate it, because he is not accused of forgetfulness; rather it is interpreted that he exerted ijtihad and his ijtihad led to the madhhab of his opponent, so he judged accordingly; thus his judgment is by his ijtihad and is valid, etc. Al-Bazzaziyya: if the judge is not a mujtahid and judges according to a fatwa, etc. And what is in al-Fath should be relied upon in the madhhab, and what is in al-Bazzaziyya is interpreted as a narration from them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the most that can be said is that this case is deemed equivalent to one who has forgotten his madhhab, and from them it has passed regarding the mujtahid that it is not valid, so the muqallid is more entitled. His statement: (meaning the imam he follows) he should have said 'the one he follows' with an added pronoun referring to the judge; the gist is that what is meant is the madhhab of the mujtahid like Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i for example, not the sultan who is muqallad (kasr lam). His statement: (And it is strange that the author of al-Bada'i' restricted the difference etc.) Its summary is that the author of al-Bada'i' made the difference regarding the validity of his ruling contrary to his madhhab in the case of a non-mujtahid judge, opposite to what is stated in al-Fath with his statement: All this is for the mujtahid judge. And the statement of al-Ramli that which preceded from Fath al-Qadir does not show that it is the reverse, and likewise his statement 'and what was restricted by him in Fath al-Qadir' reflect, it is not apparent. Then what he mentioned in al-Bada'i' is sound, because the mujtahid, if he judges contrary to his madhhab deliberately, that is a retraction from his initial madhhab due to his ijtihad changing and the necessity of following what appears to him secondarily; and if it is not known that he opposed it deliberately, he is interpreted as having done so deliberately, not that he forgot his madhhab and judged contrary to it, because the default is that he did that deliberately. This is the meaning of the statement of al-Bada'i': because he is not trusted with forgetfulness, etc. End.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the validity of a qadi's judgment when it contradicts his own madhhab. The discussion is divided into two scenarios: when the qadi is a mujtahid (capable of independent reasoning) and when he is a muqallid (follower of an imam). For the mujtahid qadi: if he judges contrary to his own opinion while forgetful of his madhhab, his judgment is valid according to Abu Hanifa (the founder of the Hanafi school). However, Abu Yusuf and al-Shaybani (the two companions) hold that it is not valid in either case (forgetful or deliberate). For deliberate opposition, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one that it is valid, another that it is not. The author notes that later authorities have differed on which view to give fatwa upon. Al-Fath al-Qadir advises that in our time, fatwa should be given according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because one who deliberately abandons his madhhab does so out of base desire, and a forgetful one was only appointed to judge by his madhhab, not another's. For the muqallid qadi (the typical Hanafi judge in later times): the general rule is that he must judge according to the madhhab of his imam (e.g., Abu Hanifa). If he does so deliberately while aware of his madhhab, his judgment is invalid and he is considered removed from office. However, the passage presents conflicting views from different Hanafi sources. Some (like al-Bazzaziyya) say that if a muqallid judge rules according to another mujtahid's madhhab, the judgment is valid and cannot be overturned by another judge, according to Muhammad, while the second (Abu Yusuf) also holds it cannot be overturned. Others (al-Qunyah) maintain that such a judgment is not valid. The author reconciles these by stating that what is in Fath al-Qadir should be relied upon: the restriction to mujtahids applies, but for muqallids, weak opinions are strengthened by the judge's ruling. The passage ends with an interesting note from al-Bada'i', who reverses the condition: for a non-mujtahid, deliberate violation invalidates, forgetfulness makes it valid according to Abu Hanifa but not according to the two companions. For a mujtahid, the judgment is valid by consensus because it is assumed he followed his ijtihad. This highlights the complexity of Hanafi positive law regarding the authority of judicial decisions.

Key Terms
مجتهد (mujtahid): A judge capable of independent legal reasoning (ijtihad), not bound to a single school.
مقلد (muqallid): A judge who follows a specific madhhab (e.g., Hanafi) and lacks the ability to perform ijtihad.
نفاذ (nufoodh): The legal effectiveness and enforceability of a qadi's judgment.
خلاف مذهب (khilaf madhhab): The act of a judge ruling contrary to the legal school (madhhab) he is bound to follow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa on the judgment of a muqallid qadi who judges contrary to his madhhab while forgetful?
A) Valid
B) Invalid
C) Valid only if the ruler approves
D) The judge is removed from office

Key Takeaway
The validity of a qadi's ruling contrary to his madhhab hinges on two factors: the judge's capacity (mujtahid/muqallid) and his state (forgetful/deliberate), and the later Hanafi tarjih favors the Sahibayn for deliberate cases to ensure adherence to the appointed madhhab.

Representatives in Lawsuits Against Absent Persons
Commentary on exception in judgment on absent persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that judgment is against the absent principal when a representative (wakil or wasi) is present, and apply the rules for proving agency and executorship, and evaluate the Hanafi position on al-musakhkhar.

Core Idea
Presence of a representative does not shift liability; judgment is against the absent principal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on an exception in judgment on absent persons. When a wakil or wasi appears, the judge rules against the absent principal or deceased, not the representative. The commentary details how agency and executorship are proven, and addresses the controversial figure of al-musakhkhar, a judge-appointed agent for the absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception and types of representatives
The base text states that if a representative attends, the exception applies. The commentary explains that the representative may be a wakil (agent) or wasi (executor), appointed either by the principal or by the Law (e.g., judge-appointed executor). The judge writes the judgment against the absent person, not the representative.
The representative does not become the defendant.
2
Understand proof requirements for agency in claims
The text from al-Bazzaziyyah outlines scenarios: a person claiming to be an agent for debt collection must prove both agency and the debt to be considered an opponent. Mere admission of agency by the defendant does not suffice. Different combinations of admission and denial are examined.
Proof (bayyinah) is required to establish agency.
3
Distinguish the rules for executorship (wasi)
The rules for executorship are similar to agency but with key differences: if the defendant admits all elements (executorship, death, property), the executor is ordered to hand over the property. If executorship is denied, the judge appoints an executor. The claimant's claim of executorship is not binding on the defendant.
Executorship is not established by mere claim.
4
Examine the debate on al-musakhkhar
Al-musakhkhar is a representative appointed by the judge on behalf of an absent person who has hidden. There is a dispute within the school: the relied-upon position (mu'tamad) is that judgment on al-musakhkhar is not permissible, following Zahir al-Din's fatwa to protect the school's stance on judgment in absentia. Khuwahrizadah permitted it for necessity. Conditions for permissibility include the absent person being within the judge's jurisdiction.
The mufta bihi is invalidity, except for necessity according to some.

حتى لو برهن على الغائب لم يقبل، وكذا لو ادعى ديناً على ميت بحضرة رجل يدعي أنه الميت وأقر المدعى عليه بالوصاية؛ كذا في آخر فصل الدعاوى . ثم رقم لآخر : القاضي لو علم أن المحضر ليس بخصم لا تسمع الخصومة المسخر لا يجوز، وقيل ينبغي أن تكون هذه المسألة على الروايتين إذ حاصله الحكم على الغائب وفيه روايتان عن أصحابنا، وكان ظهير الدين يفتي بأن الحكم على الغائب لا ينفذ كيلا يتطرقوا إلى هدم مذهب أصحابنا ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن نصب المسخر عند القائل به شرطه أن يكون الغائب في ولاية القاضي لما في الخزانة : القاضي إذا جعل نائباً عن الغائب حتى يسمع عليه الخصومة ويسمى هذا المسخر، فإذا كان الغائب ليس في ولاية هذا القاضي لا تصح هذه الإنابة وليس لهذا طريق عند علمائنا ا هـ . والمعتمد أن القضاء على المسخر لا يجوز والمجوّز له خواهرزاده لأنه أفتى بنفاذ القضاء على الغائب وهو عين القضاء على الغائب إلا لضرورة وهي في وصي
Book of Hawalah, Chapter: The Judge's Letter to Another Judge, etc. Upon him, and the opponent in the claim of sa'ayah is the commanded, not the commander if the commander is a sultan, otherwise the commander and the hirer is not an opponent for a claimant of hire or pledge or purchase like the borrower and the buyer is an opponent for all, and likewise the donee, and the opponent in the claim of the sold item before delivery is the two contracting parties, and in the corrupt sale before delivery the seller alone, and after delivery the buyer alone, and its completion will come in the Book of Claims. His statement: (except that one who stands in his place attends, like the agent and the executor) he mentioned the two examples to show that the representative may be by his deputation or by the deputation of the Law, so the executor if he is from the deceased then it is by his deputation, and if he is appointed by the judge then it is by the deputation of the Law, and the apparent meaning of the exception is that when the agent and the executor are present, the judge only judges against the absent person and against the deceased, and does not judge against the agent or the executor, and he writes in the record that he judged against the deceased and against the absent person in the presence of his agent and in the presence of his executor; thus in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: concerning the oath: if someone claims that he is the agent of an absent person for receiving a debt or property, if he provides proof of agency and the property, it is accepted. If he admits the agency but denies the property, he does not become an opponent, and evidence regarding the property is not accepted because it has not been established that he is an opponent by the admission of the defendant because it is not a proof against the claimant. If he admits the property but denies the agency, he is not asked to swear regarding the agency because the oath depends on a valid claim, and it is not found due to lack of proof of agency. And al-Khassaf mentioned that he swears regarding the agency, and the first is more correct. If he denies everything, it is like the denial of agency alone. And if he produces evidence on the property and agency, it is accepted according to Imam Abu Hanifah because the agent for receiving a debt is an opponent. And the chapter of executorship regarding property is like the chapter of agency except in one chapter: that if he claims that so-and-so the deceased appointed him as executor to preserve his property and receive it, and he has such-and-such with this present person, and the present person admits everything, he is ordered to hand over the debt and property unlike agency. If he admits the executorship and death but denies the property, he swears. If he admits the property and death but denies the executorship, the judge appoints an executor and does not make him swear because, as we mentioned, the claim of executorship is not binding. If he admits the executorship and property but denies the death, he swears him regarding his knowledge as in the case of the heir. And if he produces evidence on all of that, it is accepted in everything. End quote. And in it from the ninth [section] on appointment of executor: the opponent in establishing executorship is the adult heir or the deceased's debtor or legatee. And they differed regarding the son of the deceased: he is an opponent according to what al-Khassaf mentioned, and some scholars disagreed, and it is not established by the admission of the deceased's debtor or depositee. And if executorship is established by evidence for a claimant of debt, then another creditor or another legatee appears, the second is not judged by the evidence of the first, and according to the second [view] it is judged. In the bequest of various types of goodness, that evidence is sufficient by consensus. End quote. And he spoke generally regarding the agent, so it includes if he is an agent in litigation and claim, and if he is an agent for judgment, as when evidence is established against him, so he appoints a representative to be judged against, then he absents himself, as in al-Qunyah. And in it from the chapter of judgment on the absent: the defendant after the establishment of just evidence asked the judge for a specified period and absented himself and that period passed; if his stubbornness appears, he may judge in his absence. And similar from al-Khujandi, may Allah be pleased with him: and their condition of absence for judgment against him is a good choice: evidence was established against the agent, so he absented himself, then his principal attended or vice versa, or evidence was established against the testator, so he died and his heir attended, or evidence was established against an heir, so he absented himself and another heir attended, then in this case he judges against the one who attended with that evidence. End quote. From the book of agency: evidence of agency is not accepted from the agent for litigation without an opponent present, even if judgment was passed against him because it is a judgment in a disputed matter. End quote. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn from the fifth: if the agent for sale wants to prove his agency such that if his principal denies, his denial is not heard, he has two ways: one is that the agent hands over the property to a man, then claims he is an agent for receiving it and selling it, so he handed it to me, then the possessor says, 'I do not know his agency,' so he proves it, and the judge orders it to be handed over to him and he sells it. The second is that he says, 'This belongs to so-and-so, I sell it to you,' and when he sells it and receives its price, the buyer says, 'I will not accept delivery of the sold item because I fear that the owner will deny your agency, and perhaps the sold item will perish in my hand or decrease, so he would hold me liable,' so the agent proves that he is his agent for that, and he is compelled to accept delivery, and the authority to compel acceptance is established by evidence. And here is another way: that he sells and says, 'I am a gratuitous agent (fuduli), so I will not hand over the sold item,' so the buyer proves that he is the agent of so-and-so for sale, so he is an opponent, and it is established that he is an agent for sale. End quote. And in it also: he appointed them both to receive his debt, then the principal and one of the two agents absented themselves, so the other agent claimed, and the debtor admitted the debt but denied his agency, so the agent proved that the creditor appointed him and so-and-so the absent person to receive his debt; his agency is judged such that even if the absent person appears, he is not required to repeat the evidence. Likewise, if the debtor denied the debt and the appointment, so the present person proved both, judgment is given for the debt and for their agency. End quote. And he spoke generally regarding the agent also, so it includes if the judge appoints him on behalf of the absent person, which is called 'al-musakhkhar', and there is disagreement about it. He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: someone claimed a debt against an absent person in the presence of a man who claims he is the agent of the absent person in litigation, and the defendant admitted the agency; his admission is not valid such that if he proves [against] the absent person, it is not accepted. Likewise, if he claimed a debt against a deceased in the presence of a man who claims he is the deceased, and the defendant admitted the executorship; thus in the last chapter of claims. Then he numbered for another: if the judge knows that the present person is not an opponent, he does not hear the litigation, and judgment on the musakhkhar is not permissible. The explanation of al-musakhkhar is that the judge appoints an agent on behalf of the absent person to hear litigation against him, and it is only permissible to appoint an agent for someone who hid in his house after the judge's officer called out at the door of his house for days. Then he numbered for another: judgment on al-musakhkhar is not permissible, and it was said that this issue should be according to the two narrations, because its essence is judgment on the absent person, and there are two narrations from our companions. And Zahir al-Din used to give fatwa that judgment on the absent is not executed, lest they find a way to demolish the school of our companions. End quote. Then know that the appointment of al-musakhkhar according to the one who says it, its condition is that the absent person is within the jurisdiction of the judge, as in al-Khazanah: if the judge appoints a deputy on behalf of the absent person so that he hears litigation against him, and this is called al-musakhkhar. So if the absent person is not within the jurisdiction of this judge, this deputation is not valid, and there is no way for this according to our scholars. End quote. And the relied-upon is that judgment on al-musakhkhar is not permissible, and the one who permitted it is Khuwahrizadah, because he gave fatwa on the execution of judgment on the absent person, which is exactly judgment on the absent person except for necessity, which is in issues: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol. 7, p. 32.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining rulings related to representation in lawsuits, particularly when a judge must rule against an absent defendant. The text begins by defining who is a proper opponent in various claims (e.g., in hire, pledge, purchase). It then focuses on the exception mentioned in the base text: if a representative such as an agent (wakil) or executor (wasi) is present, the judge rules against the absent principal or deceased, not the representative. The commentary clarifies that the representative may be appointed by the principal (or deceased) or by the judge (as a legal guardian). It then cites al-Bazzaziyyah with detailed scenarios: a person claiming to be an agent for debt collection must provide proof of agency and the property; mere admission of agency without proof does not make him an opponent. The text distinguishes between claims of agency and executorship, noting that an executor's claim is not binding and has different rules. Next, the commentary discusses the appointment of a representative by the judge (al-musakhkhar) for an absent person who has hid, and the disagreement among scholars on its validity. The relied-upon position (mu'tamad) is that judgment on al-musakhkhar is not permissible, except according to Khuwahrizadah who allowed it for necessity. The condition for those who permit it is that the absent person must be within the judge's jurisdiction. The passage closes with a standard authority note.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): An agent appointed by a principal to perform a specific legal act on his behalf, such as receiving a debt or litigating.
الوصي (al-wasi): An executor appointed by a deceased person or by the judge to manage the deceased's estate and represent the estate in legal matters.
المسخر (al-musakhkhar): A representative appointed by the judge on behalf of an absent person who has hidden himself, to hear litigation against that absent person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mu'tamad) in the Hanafi school, is judgment on a judge-appointed representative (al-musakhkhar) valid?
A) Yes, it is always valid.
B) No, it is not permissible, except for necessity according to some.
C) It is valid only if the absent person is within the judge's jurisdiction.
D) It is valid only if the representative is an executor (wasi).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, representatives do not become personally liable in lawsuits against absent persons; judgment remains on the principal.
