Validity and Scope of Arbitration (Tahkim)
Ruling on arbitration: valid in non-restricted cases, invalid in hudud, qisas, diyah on aqila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for valid arbitration and identify cases where arbitration is invalid (hudud, qisas, diyah on aqila).

Core Idea
Arbitration is valid only where settlement (sulh) is valid – hudud and qisas are excluded.

Arbitration (tahkim) in Hanafi fiqh is treated like a settlement (sulh) between the parties. Its validity depends on the nature of the dispute and the capacity of the arbitrator. This lesson examines when arbitration is valid, when it is void, and the conditions that must be met, including the important intra-Hanafi disagreement over its permissibility in qisas cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm
Determine that arbitration (tahkim) is an act of appointing a third party to settle a dispute. Its hukm is sahih (valid) under certain conditions, but invalid in others.
2
Determine the scope of validity
The passage states arbitration is valid 'in other than hudud, qisas, and diyah on the aqila'. Recognise that these are cases where the parties have no authority to forgive or settle.
Arbitration is analogous to sulh; if the parties cannot settle the matter themselves, they cannot delegate it to an arbitrator.
3
List the conditions for valid arbitration
The arbitrator must be fit to be a judge (attain the qualifications of a qadi) and must base his judgment on evidence (bayyinah), confession (iqrar), or refusal to swear (nukul). He cannot judge by his own knowledge. If two arbitrators are appointed, they must agree on the ruling.
4
Identify the invalidating factors
Arbitration is void if it concerns hudud, qisas, or diyah on the aqila. Additionally, if the arbitrator judges by his own knowledge, the judgment is void.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas
Al-Khassaf prohibits arbitration in qisas, while al-Muhit permits it (considering it a right of the servant). The mufta bihi (relied-upon) view is prohibition, because qisas involves both divine and personal rights.
In practice, the view of al-Khassaf is followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكما رجلاً ليحكم بينهما فحكم ببينة أو إقرار أو نكول في غير حد وقود ودية على العاقلة صح لو صلح المحكم قاضياً لما قدمناه من الدلائل وشرط أن يكون حكمه بحجة من الثلاث ليوافق حكم الشرع وإلا يقع باطلاً وظاهره أنه لا يحكم بعلمه ولم أره في الحدود والقصاص لأن تحكيمهما بمنزلة صلحهما ولا يملكان صريحاً، ولم يصح دمهما ولذا لا يباح بالإباحة وكذا لا ولاية لهما على العاقلة فلا ينفذ حكمه عليها ولا على القاتل بالدية وحده لمخالفة النص فكان باطلاً، ولم أر حكم التحكيم في اللعان مقام الحد ولهذا قالوا : لا تقبل فيه الشهادة على الشهادة ولا كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي ولا قوله : ( واجتهاد عمر) أي حيث جعل إلقاءه الوسادة جواراً والمراد بالحالة حالة الحكومة والمراد بالأوّل الحديث السابق . مع أنه قائم قوله: (ولم أر حكم التحكيم في اللعان) قال أبو السعود: نقل الحموي عن البرجندي أن على العاقلة صح لو صلح المحكم قاضياً ولكل واحد من المحكمين أن يرجع قبل التوكيل. وقيد بكونها على العاقلة لأنها لو كانت على القاتل بأن ثبت القتل بإقراره أو ثبتت جراحة ببينة وأرشها أقل مما تتحمله العاقلة خطأ كانت الجراحة أو عمداً أو كانت قدر ما تتحمله ولكن الجراحة كانت عمداً لا توجب القصاص نفذ حكمه، وما في الكتاب من منعه في القصاص هو قول الخصاف وهو الصحيح كما في فتح القدير، وما في المحيط من جوازه فيه باعتبار أنه من حقوق العباد ضعيف رواية ودراية لأن القصاص لم يتمحض حق العبد بل هو من قبيل ما اجتمع فيه الحقان وإن كان الغالب حق العبد بدليل منع شهادة النساء فيه وكتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وقد كتبنا في الفوائد أنه كالحدود إلا في مسائل منها أن للقاضي أن يقضي به بعلمه كما في الخلاصة. وأفاد بقوله «لو صلح قاضياً» جواز تحكيم المرأة والفاسق لصلاحيتهما للقضاء، والأولى أن لا يحكما فاسقاً ولو حكما رجلين فحكم أحدهما لم يجز ولا بد من اتفاقهما على المحكوم به فلو اختلفا لم يجز كما في الولوالجية. وفي أدب القضاء للخصاف : لو قال لامرأته أنت علي حرام ونوى الطلاق دون الثلاث فحكما رجلين فحكم أحدهما بأنها بائن وحكم لآخر بأنها بائن بالثلاث لم يجز لأنهما لم يجتمعا على أمر واحد فقوله «رجلاً مثال والمراد إنساناً معلوماً فلو حكما أوّل من يدخل المسجد لم يجز إجماعاً لجهالة الصلح عليه ؛ كذا في المحيط. وأشار بصلاحيته للقضاء أن أحدهما لو وكل الحكم في الخصومة وقبل خرج عن الحكومة لتعينه خصماً في هذه الحادثة فخرج عن الشهادة فيها، ولو وكل أحدهما ابن الحكم أو من لم تقبل شهادته له لم يجز كما في المحيط وقدمنا شرائطه، وكذا ما اختاره السرخسي من جوازه في حد القذف ضعيف بالأولى لأن الغالب فيه حق الله تعالى على الأصح والحكم قال في الولوالجية : الأصح أنه لا يجوز في الحدود كلها. وشمل قوله في غير حد الخ» سائر المجتهدات من النكاح والطلاق واليمين المضافة كما سيأتي . اهـ.
If two people appoint a man to judge between them, and he judges based on evidence, confession, or refusal to swear (nukul) in other than hudud, qisas, and blood money on the aqila, it is valid if the arbitrator is fit to be a judge, due to the proofs we have presented. And it is a condition that his judgment be based on one of these three proofs to accord with Islamic law, otherwise it is void. Evidently he does not judge by his own knowledge, and I have not seen (rulings) regarding hudud and qisas because their arbitration is like their settlement, and they do not have explicit authority. Their blood is not valid, so it is not permissible by permission. Similarly, they have no authority over the aqila, so his judgment is not binding on them nor on the killer regarding blood money alone due to contravening the text, so it is void. I have not seen arbitration in li'an as a substitute for hadd, which is why they said: testimony upon testimony is not accepted in it, nor the judge's letter to another judge. And his statement: (and the ijtihad of Umar) meaning when he made the spreading of the cushion a shelter; and the intended state is the state of judgment, and the first refers to the earlier hadith. While it is established. His statement: (I have not seen arbitration in li'an). Abu al-Sa'ud said: al-Hamawi transmitted from al-Birjandi that on the aqila it is valid if the arbitrator is fit to be a judge, and each of the two arbitrators may retract before appointment. And the restriction to it being on the aqila because if it were on the killer – if the killing is established by his confession or a wound is established by evidence and its compensation is less than what the aqila bears, whether the wound was accidental or intentional, or it was an amount they could bear but the wound was intentional not requiring retaliation, then his judgment is executed. What is in the book prohibiting it in qisas is the statement of al-Khassaf, and it is the correct view as in Fath al-Qadir. What is in al-Muhit permitting it there because it is considered among the rights of the people is weak in transmission and reasoning, because qisas is not purely a right of the servant but rather is of the type where both rights are combined, even if the right of the servant predominates, evidenced by the prohibition of women's testimony in it and the judge's letter to another judge. We have written in al-Fawa'id that it is like hudud except in certain issues, including that the judge may rule by his own knowledge as in al-Khulasa. And his statement 'if fit to be a judge' indicates the permissibility of appointing a woman and a fasiq as arbitrators because they are fit for judgment, but it is better not to appoint a fasiq. If two men are appointed and one of them judges, it is not valid; they must agree on the ruling, and if they differ it is not valid, as in al-Walwalijiyya. In Adab al-Qada by al-Khassaf: If a man said to his wife 'You are forbidden to me' intending divorce less than three, and they appointed two men, one ruled she is irrevocably divorced and the other that she is irrevocably divorced with three, it is not valid because they did not unite on one matter. His statement 'a man' is an example meaning a known person. If they appointed the first person to enter the mosque, it is not valid by consensus due to the ignorance of the settlement, as in al-Muhit. And his statement indicates that if one of them delegates the judgment in the case and accepts, he leaves the position of judgment because he becomes a party in this incident, thus leaving testimony in it. If one of them delegates the son of the judge or someone whose testimony is not accepted, it is not valid, as in al-Muhit, and we have presented its conditions. Similarly, what al-Sarakhsi chose regarding its permissibility in hadd of slander is weak by priority, because the right of Allah predominates in it on the correct view. The judge said in al-Walwalijiyya: the correct view is that it is not permissible in all hudud. And his statement 'in other than hudud' includes all ijtihad-based matters of marriage, divorce, and suspended oaths, as will come.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states the ruling on arbitration (tahkim). The commentator explains that arbitration is valid for non-hudud, non-qisas, and diyah not on the aqila, provided the arbitrator judges based on evidence (bayyinah), confession (iqrar), or refusal to swear (nukul). The ruling is analogous to settlement (sulh) because the parties have the authority to compromise. The text emphasizes that the arbitrator cannot judge based on his own knowledge, and arbitration in hudud and qisas is invalid because these involve the rights of Allah and require a regular judge. For diyah on the aqila, arbitration is also invalid because the aqila are not parties to the case. The passage then discusses the permissibility of appointing women and fasiqs as arbitrators, and the requirement that two arbitrators must agree on the ruling. If they disagree, the arbitration is null. The commentator also clarifies that qisas is not purely a personal right, so arbitration is invalid in qisas. The correct position is that of al-Khassaf prohibiting arbitration in qisas.

Key Terms
تحكيم (tahkim): Arbitration: appointment of a third party by disputants to settle their case.
محكم (muhakkam): The arbitrator appointed by the parties to judge between them.
نكول (nukul): Refusal of the defendant to take an oath after the plaintiff demands it; considered an admission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, why is arbitration (tahkim) invalid in cases of hudud and qisas?
A) Because the arbitrator must be a qualified judge appointed by the ruler.
B) Because hudud and qisas involve the right of Allah and cannot be delegated to arbitration.
C) Because the parties cannot forgive hudud and qisas.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration in Hanafi law is limited to cases where settlement is permissible; it is invalid in hudud, qisas, and diyah on aqila.

Retraction of Arbitrators Before Judgment: The General Ruling and Qiyas to Agency
Ruling on retraction and binding effect of arbitration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on retraction of an arbitrator before judgment, apply the qiyas to agency, and distinguish the binding scope of the arbitrator's judgment.

Core Idea
An arbitrator may be retracted before judgment; once judgment is given, it binds only the parties.

In Hanafi fiqh, arbitration derives its authority from the consent of the parties. This lesson examines the scope of that authority, focusing on when an arbitrator can be dismissed and the binding effect of his judgment. Using qiyas to agency, the Hanafi school clarifies that retraction is permitted before the verdict but not after. Additional rulings clarify that the judgment does not extend to third parties and that some matters require a regular judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling on: the appointment of an arbitrator and the right to retract. The ruling is that each party may dismiss the arbitrator before judgment because the authority is delegated from both parties, similar to revocable contracts.
The act is arbitration (tahkim), and the key ruling is the permissibility of retraction (azl).
2
Locate the dalil
The textual proof is the qiyas (analogy) to agency (wakalah), mudarabah, and partnership. These contracts are revocable before execution because they are based on mutual consent and delegation.
The dalil is qiyas jaliy, as stated in the text: 'like mudarabah, partnership, and agency'.
3
Analyze the qiyas
Identify the asl (agency), the far' (arbitration), and the illah (both are revocable before completion because authority is derived from the parties). Confirm that the illah is jaliyyah (clear), extracted via tanqih al-manat.
The objectors argue that arbitration is a form of judgment, but the Hanafi response distinguishes the arbitrator's authority from that of a regular judge.
4
Note the binding effect after judgment
Once the arbitrator issues a judgment, it becomes binding on the parties because it emanates from a valid legal authority (wilayah shar'iyyah). Retraction after judgment is not possible.
The text states: 'If he judges, it is binding on them.'
5
Distinguish the scope and special cases
The judgment only binds the disputing parties, not third parties. Special cases: annulment of suspended oaths requires a regular judge (the Hanafi companions refrained from issuing fatwa for the arbitrator), and the arbitrator cannot perform li'an.
Example of sale defect: the arbitrator's ruling for return does not allow the seller to reclaim from his own seller without consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولكل واحد من الحكمين أن يرجع قبل حكمه لأنه تقلد من جهتهما فكان لكل منهما عزله وهو من الأمور الجائزة فينفرد أحدهما بنقضه كالمضاربة والشركة والوكالة قوله : (فإن حكم لزمهما لصدوره عن ولاية شرعية فلا يبطل حكمه بعزلهما. وأشار بقوله «لزمهما» إلى أنه لا يتعدى إلى غيرهما فلو حكماه في عيب مبيع فقضى برده ليس للبائع أن يرده على بائعه إلا أن يرضى البائع الأول والثاني والمشترى على تحكيمه ؛ كذا في فتح القدير . وفي الولوالجية : حكم المحكم في فسخ اليمين المضافة الصحيح أنه ينفذ لأنه فيما بينهما بمنزلة القاضي المولى وإن كانا يفترقان في شيء آخر لكن هذا شيء يعلم ولا يفتى به ا هـ . . وفي السراج الوهاج إلا أن أصحابنا امتنعوا من هذه الفتوى وقالوا لا بد فيها من حكم المولى المحكم ليس له أن يلا عن بين الزوجين قوله : (قال رضي الله عنه نفاذ قضائه صحيح) الذي في القنية
His statement: (And each of the two arbitrators may retract before his judgment because he has undertaken from both of them, so each has the power to dismiss him, and it is among permissible matters; therefore one of them may individually annul it, like mudarabah, partnership, and agency). His statement: (If he judges, it is binding on them because it issues from a legal authority, so his judgment is not nullified by their dismissal). And by his statement 'binding on them' he indicates that it does not extend to others. So if they appointed him regarding a defect in a sold item and he ruled for its return, the seller cannot return it to his own seller unless the first seller, the second seller, and the buyer agree to his arbitration. As in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Walwalijiyya: The judgment of the arbitrator regarding the annulment of a suspended oath: the correct view is that it is executed because he is like a regular judge between them, although they differ in other aspects. But this is known and fatwa is not given on it. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, except that our companions refrained from this fatwa and said a regular judge's ruling is necessary in it. The arbitrator is not qualified to perform li'an between spouses. His statement: (He said, may Allah be pleased with him: His judgment is valid) as in al-Qunyah.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the procedure of arbitration. It first states that each of the two arbitrators (if multiple) has the right to retract before issuing judgment. This is because the arbitrator derives authority from both parties, and each party can dismiss him. The commentator analogizes this to other permissive contracts like mudarabah and agency where either party can terminate before execution. However, once the arbitrator gives a judgment, it becomes binding on both parties and cannot be nullified even if they later dismiss him, because at the time of judgment he had valid authority. The binding effect is limited to the parties involved; it does not extend to third parties. For example, if two parties appoint an arbitrator to judge a sale defect, his decision that the item must be returned only binds them, not the original seller of the seller. The passage also notes a dispute about the arbitrator's power to annul suspended oaths: some companions refrained from giving fatwa on it, requiring instead a regular judge. Similarly, an arbitrator cannot perform li'an (mutual imprecation) between spouses. The text concludes by affirming that the ruling of an arbitrator, if valid, is executable.

Key Terms
عزل (azl): Dismissal/retraction of the arbitrator by the parties before judgment.
تحكيم (tahkim): Arbitration; appointment of a private individual to resolve a dispute.
ولاية شرعية (wilayah shar'iyyah): Legal authority; the basis for the binding nature of the arbitrator's judgment after it is issued.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does an arbitrator's judgment become binding?
A) Immediately upon appointment.
B) Only after both parties accept the judgment.
C) As soon as the judgment is issued, and it cannot be nullified by later retraction.
D) It is never binding; the parties can always disregard it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an arbitrator may be retracted before judgment, but once judgment is given, it is binding on the parties and does not extend to third parties.

The Non-Transitivity of an Arbitrator's Ruling: Four Rules and an Exception
Enumerates cases where arbitrator's ruling does not extend beyond parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four rules limiting the arbitrator's ruling to consenting parties and identify the partnership exception.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's ruling binds only those who consented, unlike a judge's ruling, except when it resembles a merchant's compromise (sulh).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents four specific applications of a fundamental Hanafi principle: the arbitrator (muhakkam) lacks general authority. Unlike a judge, his ruling does not extend to non-consenting parties. We will examine each rule and the sole exception involving partners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Understand that the arbitrator's ruling is binding only on those who voluntarily submitted to his arbitration. This is the key distinction from a judge's ruling.
The principle is rooted in the arbitrator's lack of general authority (wilaya 'amma).
2
Parse Each Rule (11th-14th)
For each of the four rules, identify the involved parties and why the ruling does not extend. The 11th rule involves an heir and the deceased/the other heirs; the 12th involves a buyer and his seller's seller; the 13th involves an agent and his principal; the 14th involves a guardian and a minor.
In all cases, the missing element is consent from the party not bound.
3
Understand the Rationale
The reasoning is that the arbitrator's authority derives solely from the consent of those who appointed him. Unlike a judge, he does not have jurisdiction over others.
This is a clear contrast highlighted by the phrase 'بخلاف حكم القاضي' (unlike the judge's ruling).
4
Note the Exception for Partners
The exception: if one partner and a creditor appoint an arbitrator, his ruling against that partner extends to the absent partner. The reason is that the ruling is analogous to a compromise (sulh), which merchants are deemed to accept by custom.
This exception is specific to partnership disputes involving joint property.
5
Contrast with a Judge's Ruling
A judge's ruling is transitive because he holds general authority over all Muslims. The arbitrator's ruling lacks this transitivity except in the partnership case where custom implies consent.
This contrast reinforces the core principle and its practical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادية عشرة ما في شرح التلخيص أنه لا يتعدى حكمه من وارث إلى الباقي والميت حتى لو ادعى عند المحكم رجل على وارث بدين على الميت وأقام بينة فحكم له بما ادعاه على ذلك الوارث لم يكن حكماً على بقية الورثة ولا على الميت لعدم رضاهم بتحكيمه بخلاف حكم القاضي. الثانية عشرة لا يتعدى حكمه بالعيب من المشتري على بائعه إلا برضا بائع بائعه كما في المحيط . الثالثة عشرة لا يتعدى حكمه على وكيل بعيب المبيع إلى موكله وهما في فتح القدير. الرابعة عشرة لا يصح حكمه على وصي صغير بما فيه ضرر عليه لما في البزازية : وإذا حكم الوصي على الصغير ومن يدعي عليه الوصي مال الصغير فحكم بما هو ضرر على الصغير لا بمنزلة صلح الوصي وإن كان في حكمه نفع للصغير يصح حكمه ا هـ . يصح ثم اعلم أن حكم المحكم لا يتعدى إلى غير المحكوم عليه إلا في مسألة مذكورة في التلخيص وشرحه : لو حكم أحد الشريكين وغريم له رجلاً فحكم بينهما وألزم الشريك شيئاً . المال من المشترك نفذ حكمه على الشريك وتعدى إلى الغائب لأن حكمه بمنزلة الصلح في حق الشريك الغائب والصلح من صنيع التجار فكان كل واحد من الشريكين راضياً بالصلح وما في معناه.
The eleventh: What is in Sharh al-Talkhis: His ruling does not extend from an heir to the remaining heirs or to the deceased, so if a man sues an heir for a debt of the deceased before an arbitrator and produces evidence, and he rules for him what he claimed against that heir, it is not a ruling against the rest of the heirs nor against the deceased, due to their lack of consent to his arbitration, unlike the ruling of a judge. The twelfth: His ruling regarding a defect does not extend from the buyer to his seller except with the consent of the seller's seller, as in al-Muhit. The thirteenth: His ruling against an agent regarding a defect of the sold item does not extend to his principal, and the two are in Fath al-Qadir. The fourteenth: His ruling against a guardian of a minor regarding harm to him is not valid, as in al-Bazzaziyyah: 'And when the guardian rules against the minor and the one who sues the guardian for the minor's property, and he rules what is harmful to the minor, it is not considered a compromise by the guardian, but if in his ruling there is benefit for the minor, his ruling is valid.' Then know that the ruling of the arbitrator does not extend to anyone other than the one against whom it is given, except in a case mentioned in al-Talkhis and its commentary: If one of two partners and his creditor appoint a man as arbitrator, and he rules between them and obliges the partner with something of the joint property, his ruling is executed against that partner and extends to the absent partner, because his ruling is like a compromise regarding the absent partner, and compromise is a practice of merchants, so each of the two partners is satisfied with the compromise and matters analogous to it.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents four specific applications of a core principle: an arbitrator's ruling is binding only on those who consented to his arbitration, unlike a judge whose ruling extends to all. The eleventh rule: if an heir is sued for a debt of the deceased, the arbitrator's ruling only binds that heir, not other heirs or the deceased. The twelfth: a buyer cannot use the arbitrator's ruling against defect to sue the seller unless the seller's seller also consented. The thirteenth: a ruling against an agent does not bind the principal. The fourteenth: an arbitrator cannot validly rule against a guardian of a minor in a harmful way; if beneficial, it is valid. Then the exception: in a partnership, if one partner and a creditor appoint an arbitrator, the ruling against that partner extends to the absent partner because it is analogous to a compromise, which merchants are deemed to accept.

Key Terms
محكم (muhakkam): A private arbitrator chosen by disputing parties to settle their dispute.
صلح (sulh): A compromise agreement between parties to settle a dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does an arbitrator's ruling against one partner extend to the absent partner in a partnership dispute?
A) Because the absent partner is an heir.
B) Because it is analogous to a compromise (sulh) which merchants are deemed to accept.
C) Because the arbitrator has general authority like a judge.
D) Because the partner is a guardian.

Key Takeaway
An arbitrator's ruling is binding only on those who consented to his appointment, except in partnerships where it is treated as a merchant's compromise.

The Qadi's Universal Ruling vs. Arbitrator's Limited Authority
States the universal binding effect of a qadi's ruling in four status matters, and the arbitrator's lack of such effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four matters where a qadi's ruling binds universally and explain why an arbitrator's ruling does not, including the practical consequences for claims of ownership.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling on status (freedom, lineage, marriage, patronage) binds all; an arbitrator's only binds the disputing parties.

In Hanafi law, not all judicial decisions carry the same weight. This passage draws a sharp distinction between a qadi (appointed judge) and a muhakkam (arbitrator): the former's ruling on certain personal status matters extends to everyone, while the latter's is confined to the parties in the dispute. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a ruling affects third parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four exceptional matters
The text lists four matters—freedom (hurriyyah), lineage (nasab), marriage (nikah), and patronage (wala')—in which the qadi's ruling is universally binding. Note that these are fundamental personal status issues.
These four are considered 'qat'i' in effect due to their public nature.
2
Contrast with the arbitrator's position
The author states that the arbitrator's ruling in these matters does not extend beyond the parties. This means a third party can still raise a claim, e.g., claiming ownership of a person the arbitrator declared free.
3
Analyze the consequence for ownership claims
If an arbitrator frees a slave, a third party may still sue that person as a slave. But if a qadi freed him, the claim would be rejected because the qadi's ruling is conclusive for all.
This illustrates the practical difference in legal effect.
4
Examine ancillary issues: imprisonment and gifts
The author opines that an arbitrator should not imprison (disagreeing with Sadr al-Shari'ah) and that it is permissible for him to accept gifts and invitations, except if the gift is timed to influence his judgment.
These points reflect the author's own view (Ibn Nujaym).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنهم قالوا إن القضاء يتعدى إلى الكافة في أربع : الحرية والنسب والنكاح والولاء، ولم يصرحوا بحكمها من المحكم. ويجب أن لا يتعدى فتسمع دعوى الملك في المحكوم بعتقه من المحكم بخلاف القاضي، وينبغي أن لا يلي المحكم الحبس ولم أره. وكذا لم أر حكم قبوله الهدية وإجابة الدعوة وينبغي أن يجوزا له الانتهاء التحكيم بالفراغ إلا أن يهدى إليه وقته من أحدهما فينبغي أن لا يجوز
Then know that they said: The qada' (judicial ruling) extends to everyone in four matters: freedom, lineage, marriage, and wala'. And they did not explicitly state its ruling from the arbitrator. And it must not extend, so a claim of ownership is heard against the person ruled to be free by the arbitrator, unlike the judge. And it is appropriate that the arbitrator should not carry out imprisonment, and I have not seen it. Similarly, I have not seen his ruling on accepting gifts and responding to invitations, and it is appropriate that they should be permissible for him, except that if a gift is given to him during his time from one of the parties, then it should not be permissible.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage highlights a key distinction between a judge and an arbitrator: a judge's ruling on matters of personal status (freedom, lineage, marriage, and patronage) binds all Muslims universally. An arbitrator's ruling does not have this effect. For example, if an arbitrator rules that a person is free, a claim of ownership against that person is still heard, whereas if a judge had so ruled, such a claim would be rejected. Additionally, the author discusses ancillary issues: it is appropriate that the arbitrator should not have the power to imprison (though the margin suggests Sadr al-Shari'ah allowed it), and it is permissible for an arbitrator to accept gifts and invitations from the parties, except if the gift is specifically timed to influence his judgment.

Key Terms
الحرية (al-hurriyyah): The status of being free as opposed to enslaved.
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage or parentage.
النكاح (al-nikah): Marriage.
الولاء (al-wala'): Patronage (the bond between a freed slave and his former master).
المحكم (al-muhakkam): Arbitrator, a private judge appointed by the parties to resolve a dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an arbitrator rules that a person is free, what happens if someone else later claims ownership of that person?
A) The claim is rejected because the arbitrator's ruling is binding on all.
B) The claim is heard because the arbitrator's ruling does not extend universally.
C) The claim goes to a judge automatically.
D) The claim is rejected unless the arbitrator consents.

Key Takeaway
A qadi's ruling on freedom, lineage, marriage, and patronage binds everyone, but an arbitrator's ruling on these matters only binds the parties.

Arbitrator's Jurisdiction and Witness Discrepancies in Hanafi Law
Rulings with reasoning on arbitrator's authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that an arbitrator may rule in any location, apply the rule on witness discrepancies in agency for litigation based on the identity of the goal (qada'), and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on imprisoning a recalcitrant party.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's jurisdiction is not geographically restricted; conflicting witness statements about the appointed qadi are accepted if the judicial function is identical.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq highlights two unique aspects of arbitration compared to litigation before a judge. First, the arbitrator's jurisdiction is not geographically restricted to the place of arbitration. Second, when witnesses testify about the terms of agency for litigation, discrepancies that do not affect the underlying judicial function are overlooked. The passage also addresses a debated question: can the arbitrator imprison a party who refuses to comply? We will analyze the reasoning behind these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct rulings
The passage presents the fifteenth and sixteenth points of difference between an arbitrator and a judge. Note the rulings: (15) arbitrator is not limited to the country of arbitration; (16) witness discrepancy about the city of the qadi is accepted, but not about a specific faqih.
These are two separate legal rules with their own rationale.
2
Analyze the territorial jurisdiction rule
Understand why the arbitrator is not restricted to a single location. The reasoning is that the essence of arbitration is the same judicial function (qada') regardless of location. The parties' agreement to arbitrate implies consent to the arbitrator's authority globally, unless restricted by contract.
This differs from a judge who has a limited territorial jurisdiction.
3
Examine the witness discrepancy rule
When witnesses disagree on whether the agency was to the qadi of Kufa or Basra, the testimony is accepted because the goal (qada') is identical. However, if they disagree on a specific faqih (e.g., Faqih Zayd vs Faqih Umar), the testimony is rejected because the choice is personal and affects the agency.
The key is whether the difference changes the underlying function.
4
Trace the jurisprudential reasoning (ta'lil)
The ruling rests on the principle that restrictions are only considered when beneficial. In the case of the qadi, the location is irrelevant to the judicial function, so the divergence is meaningless. For a specific faqih, the identity matters, so the testimony must be consistent.
This illustrates the Hanafi method of ta'lil (ratiocination) in deriving rulings.
5
Evaluate the imprisonment issue
The author (Ibn Nujaym) initially stated the arbitrator should not imprison. However, Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly allowed it as a means to enforce the ruling. The commentary notes that the latter view is recorded and considered mufta bihi.
This shows intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the reliance on later authorities (mutakhkhirin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة عشرة لا يتقيد ببلد التحكيم وله الحكم في البلاد كلها كما في المحيط . السادس عشر مما خالف فيه المحكم القاضي لو اختلف الشاهدان فشهد أحدهما أنه وكله بخصومة فلان إلى قاضي الكوفة والآخر إلى قاضي البصرة تقبل، ولو شهد أحدهما بذلك إلى الفقيه فلان فشهد الآخر به إلى الفقيه فلان آخر لم تقبل كما في أدب. القضاء للخصاف من باب الشهادة على الوكالة والفرق في شرحه للصدر الشهيد . قوله : (وينبغي أن لا يلي المحكم الحبس) قدمنا أوّل فصل الحبس أن صدر الشريعة صرح يليه ووجد في بعض النسخ قبل قوله ولم أره) ما نصه وفي صدر الشريعة من باب التحكيم قال : وفائدة إلزام الخصم أن المتبايعين إن حكما حكماً فالحكم يجبر المشتري على تسليم الثمن والبائع على تسليم المبيع ومن امتنع بحبسه .اهـ. فهذا صريح في أن الحكم يحبس .اهـ وكأنه وجد بعد أو المراد ولم هذه النسخة أره لغيره تأمل قوله : (السادس عشر إلى آخر القولة وجد في بعض النسخ كما في الخامس عشر ووجد في بعضها في آخر القولة الآتية والأولى أصوب قوله : (والفرق في شرحه للصدر الشهيد) وهو أن الوكيل بالخصومة إلى قاضي الكوفة يكون وكيلاً بها إلى قاضي البصرة وكذا العكس لأن المطلوب نفس القضاء ولا يختلف والتقييد إنما يراعى إذا كان مفيداً وحكم الحكم توسط
The fifteenth: He is not restricted to the country of arbitration, and he has the right to rule in all lands, as in al-Muhit. The sixteenth: Among the matters where the arbitrator differs from the judge: if the two witnesses differ, one testifying that he appointed him for litigation to the qadi of Kufa and the other to the qadi of Basra, it is accepted; but if one testifies that he appointed him to faqih so-and-so and the other to faqih so-and-so else, it is not accepted, as in Adab al-Qada for al-Khassaf, from the chapter on testimony on agency, and the difference is in its commentary by al-Sadr al-Shahid. His saying: (It is appropriate that the arbitrator should not carry out imprisonment). We presented at the beginning of the chapter on imprisonment that Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly allowed it, and found in some copies before his saying (and I have not seen it) the text: And in Sadr al-Shari'ah from the chapter on arbitration he said: The benefit of compelling the opponent is that if the two parties appoint an arbitrator, the arbitrator compels the buyer to pay the price and the seller to deliver the sold item, and if one refuses, he imprisons him. Thus it is explicit that the arbitrator imprisons. It is as if he found it later, or the meaning of (and I have not seen it in this copy) is for someone else. Consider his saying: (The sixteenth to the end of the statement) was found in some copies as in the fifteenth, and found in some at the end of the following statement, and the first is more correct. His saying: (And the difference in its commentary by al-Sadr al-Shahid) is that the agent for litigation to the qadi of Kufa is also an agent for litigation to the qadi of Basra, and vice versa, because the goal is the same qada' and it does not differ, and the restriction is considered only if it is beneficial, and the ruling of the arbitrator is intermediate.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage contains two more points of difference between the arbitrator and the judge. Fifteenth: the arbitrator is not limited to a specific territory; he can rule in any location. Sixteenth: regarding witness testimony about agency to a specific qadi, if witnesses differ on the city (Kufa vs. Basra) the testimony is accepted because the goal is the same judicial function; but if they differ on the specific faqih, it is not accepted because the selection is personal and the arbitrator's authority is more restricted. The commentary addresses the imprisonment issue: though the author previously said the arbitrator should not imprison, Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly states that the arbitrator can imprison a recalcitrant party, as part of enforcing his decision. The author notes this discrepancy.

Key Terms
بلد التحكيم (balad al-tahkim): The country or city where the arbitration agreement was made.
شاهدان (shahidan): Two witnesses testifying about the terms of the agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the qadis are brothers.
B) Because the goal is the same judicial function, regardless of location.
C) Because the arbitrator can rule in any city.
D) Because the witnesses are unreliable anyway.

Key Takeaway
An arbitrator's authority is flexible: jurisdiction is global, and minor discrepancies in witness testimony about the appointed qadi are overlooked when the judicial function is unchanged.

Lower Floor Owner's Right to Insert Peg or Window: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on lower owner's right to modify shared wall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the lower owner's right to modify the wall and apply the qiyas argument that underpins Abu Hanifa's prohibition.

Core Idea
A property right of one owner can restrict another's freedom to use his own property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute: may the owner of a lower floor drive a peg or make a window in his own wall without the upper owner's consent? The disagreement hinges on whether the upper owner has a 'respected right' attached to the shared structure, analogous to a mortgagee's right. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi jurists balance ownership rights and mutual obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling dispute
The lower owner wants to insert a peg (watad) or make a window (kuwwah) in his own wall that supports the upper floor. The question is whether this requires the upper owner's consent.
The wall is part of the lower owner's property but also structurally supports the upper floor.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his qiyas argument
Abu Hanifa holds that any insertion without consent is prohibited (haram). He analogizes to the case of a mortgagee or lessee whose attached right prevents the owner from disposing of the property freely.
The illa (effective cause) is that a respected right of another is attached to the property.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their permissibility argument
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the lower owner may do whatever does not harm the upper floor. Their original principle (asl) is permissibility (ibaha) because it is his own property.
They reject the presumption of harm and require actual harm before prohibition.
4
Examine the illa and the qiyas structure
Abu Hanifa's qiyas uses the mortgagee/lessee as asl: their right restricts the owner. The far' is the upper owner's right in the wall. The illa is the 'attached respected right'. The Sahibayn contest the existence of a respected right without proven harm.
The debate is about whether the upper owner's interest rises to the level of a right that restricts the lower owner's absolute ownership.
5
Consider the practical consequence and mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa practice relies on Abu Hanifa's view for caution (ihtiyat), but the Sahibayn's view is also considered valid. The practical ruling: without consent, the lower owner should not insert even a small nail unless it is clearly harmless.
The text indicates that opening a door is prohibited by consensus, showing that at some point harm is presumed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل شتى أي متفرقات من كتاب القضاء جرياً على عادة المؤلفين جمع شتيت كمرضى جمع مريض من أمر شت أي متفرق وشت الأمر شتاً وشتاتاً ،تفرق واشتت مثله، والشتيت المتفرق، وقوم شتى وأشياء شتى وجاؤوا أشتاتاً أي متفرقين وأنكر الأصمعي أن تقول شتان ما بينهما وما ورد منه فمولد وتمامه في الصحاح ومنه قوله تعالى إن سعيكم لشتى [الليل : ٤] أي إن عملكم لمختلف أي في الجزاء وفي الرازي الكبير أنها أنزلت في أبي بكر وأبي سفيان، وفي الدر المنثور في صاحب نخلة كان غصن منها متدلياً في بيت فقير فكان إذا جاء لينثر ثمره وسقط شيء منها في بيت جاره يأخذه الصبيان فكان ينزل إليهم ويأخذه منهم حتى كان يأخذ التمرة من فم الصبي فشكا إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فدعا صاحب النخلة وقال له أعطني نخلتك المائلة ولك نخلة في الجنة فقال : يا رسول الله ليس لي ثمرة أطيب منها فذهب وكان عندهما كلامهما فذهب إليه واشترى منه النخله بأربعين نخلة على ساق واحد وأشهد له رجل يسمع ثم جاء إلى رسول الله له وأعطاه النخلة فأرسل النبي خلف الفقير وأعطاه النخلة قوله : (لا يتدذ وسفل ولا يثقب فيه كوة بلا رضا ذي العلو) أي عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يفعل ما لا يضر بالعلو. وقيل: ما حكي عنهما تفسير لقوله فلا خلاف. وقيل: بل فيه خلاف فعندهما الأصل الإباحة لأنه تصرف في ملكه وهو يقتضي الإطلاق، والأصل عنده الحظر لأنه تعلق به حق محترم للغير فصار كحق المرتهن والمستأجر في منع المالك عن التصرف فيه والإطلاق يعارضه الرضا فإذا أشكل لا يزول المنع على أنه لا يعرى عن نوع ضرر بالعلو من توهين البناء أو نقصه فيمنع عنه ولهذا لا يملك صاحب السفل أن يهدم كل الجدار أو السقف وكذا بعضه، وقول أبي حنيفة قياس كما ذكره فخر الإسلام وفي المغرب وتدا لوتد ضربه بالميتدة وأثبته. وفي البناية أنه كالخازوق وهو القطعة من الخشب أو الحديد يدق في الحائط ليعلق عليه شيء أو يربط به شيء ء اهـ. والكوة - بفتح الكاف - ثقب البيت والجمع كوى وقد تضم الكاف في المفرد والجمع، ويستعار لمفاتيح الماء إلى المزارع والجداول ؛ كذا في المغرب. وفي الصحاح أن الجمع يمد ويقصر. وأشار المصنف إلى منعه من فتح الباب ووضع الجذوع وهدم سفله. وفي فتح القدير أن فتح الباب ينبغي أن يمنع اتفاقاً وإن وضع مسماراً صغيراً أو
Miscellaneous issues, i.e., scattered matters from the Book of Judiciary, following the custom of authors to collect scattered things like 'marda' as plural of 'marid' from 'amr shatt' meaning scattered. And 'shatta' the matter became scattered, and 'ishtatta' is similar. And 'al-shatit' is the scattered one. And 'qawm shatta' and 'ashya' shatta' and 'jau ashataan' meaning scattered. And al-Asma'i denied saying 'shatana ma baynahuma' and what is reported from it is a neologism. Its full explanation is in al-Sihah. From it is the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'Indeed your efforts are diverse' (al-Layl:4), meaning your deeds are different in recompense. In al-Razi al-Kabir, it was revealed about Abu Bakr and Abu Sufyan. In al-Durr al-Manthur, about the owner of a palm tree whose branch was hanging over a poor man's house. When he came to shake off its fruit, some of it fell into the neighbor's house, and the boys would take it. He would come down to them and take it from them, until he would take the date from the child's mouth. He complained to the Prophet ﷺ, and the Prophet called the owner of the palm tree and said: 'Give me your leaning palm tree and you will have a palm tree in Paradise.' He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, I have no fruit better than it.' He went, and they had some conversation, then he went to him and bought the palm tree for forty palm trees on a single trunk, and he had a man who heard as a witness. Then he came to the Messenger of Allah and gave him the palm tree. The Prophet sent after the poor man and gave him the palm tree. His statement: (He does not insert a peg in the lower floor nor make a window in it without the consent of the owner of the upper floor) i.e., according to Abu Hanifah. And the two said: He may do what does not harm the upper floor. It is said that what is reported from them is an interpretation of his statement, so there is no disagreement. And it is said: Rather there is disagreement for them: the original principle is permissibility because it is a disposal in his property and it requires absolute freedom. And the original principle for him (Abu Hanifah) is prohibition because a respected right of another person is attached to it, so it becomes like the right of the mortgagee and the lessee in preventing the owner from disposing in it. Absolute freedom is opposed by consent, and if it is problematic, the prohibition is not removed, and it is not free from some type of harm to the upper floor, such as weakening the building or its reduction, so it is prohibited. Therefore, the owner of the lower floor does not have the right to demolish the entire wall or ceiling, nor part of it. And the statement of Abu Hanifah is qiyas as mentioned by Fakhr al-Islam. In al-Mughrib: 'watada' means he drove a peg with a mallet and fixed it. In al-Binayah, it is like a post, which is a piece of wood or iron driven into the wall to hang something on or to tie something to, end quote. And 'al-kuwwah' (with fatha on kaf) is an opening in the house, plural 'kuwan', and the kaf may be damma in singular and plural. It is used metaphorically for water channels to fields and streams; likewise in al-Mughrib. In al-Sihah, the plural can be long or short. And the author indicated its prohibition for opening a door, placing beams, and demolishing his lower floor. In Fath al-Qadir, opening a door should be prohibited unanimously, even if he places a small nail or
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage begins the 'Miscellaneous Issues' (Masail Shatta) from the Book of Judiciary, following the authors' habit of collecting scattered rulings. After a linguistic explanation of 'shatta' and a story about a palm tree related to the verse 'Indeed your efforts are diverse', the text presents the core dispute: the owner of a lower floor (sifl) may not insert a peg (watad) or make a window (kuwwah) in his wall without the consent of the upper floor owner ('ulu) according to Abu Hanifah. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that he may do what does not harm the upper floor. The commentary explains two interpretations: either there is no disagreement (the Sahibayn's view being a clarification), or there is genuine disagreement. According to Abu Hanifah, the original principle (asl) is prohibition because the upper owner has a respected right attached to the wall, similar to the right of a mortgagee or lessee preventing the owner from disposing of the property. The Sahibayn hold that the original principle is permissibility (ibaha) since it is one's own property, but this is restricted by harm. Abu Hanifah's view is based on qiyas (analogy) as noted by Fakhr al-Islam. Definitions of peg and window are given from lexicons. The author also indicates that the same prohibition applies to opening a door, placing beams, or demolishing the lower floor. The ruling is that even a small nail is subject to the dispute, though some later texts (Fath al-Qadir) suggest opening a door is prohibited unanimously.

Key Terms
وتد (watad): A peg, stake, or nail driven into a wall to hang something.
كوة (kuwwah): An opening or window in a wall, also used for water channels.
سفل (sifl): The lower floor or ground floor of a building, usually owned by one person.
علو ('ulu): The upper floor or story above the lower floor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa prohibits any modification of a shared wall without consent due to the upper owner's attached right, while the Sahibayn allow what causes no harm; the mufta bihi often follows Abu Hanifa for caution.

Rights of the Upper Floor Owner: When May He Build?
Ruling with proof on building in upper floor with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the upper owner's right to build and apply the mufta bihi rule based on certainty of harm.

Core Idea
An owner may build only when harm is certainly avoided; uncertainty denies the right.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical property dispute: the owner of an upper floor wants to build but the lower owner fears damage. The Hanafi school has multiple positions. This lesson unpacks the ikhtilaf and identifies the mufta bihi, which hinges on whether harm is certain or uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ownership
Recognize that the act is building in the upper floor (ulu) owned separately from the lower floor (sufl). Ownership grants a prima facie right to use the property.
Separate ownership of floors is recognized in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Map the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Identify the three positions: (1) absolute permission as long as no harm, (2) absolute prohibition regardless of harm, (3) the chosen (mukhtar) position: permission only if harm is certainly absent.
The ikhtilaf is among the mashayikh (later scholars) on the interpretation of Abu Hanifa's view.
3
Understand the Basis of the Chosen Position
The chosen position (al-mukhtar li al-fatwa) balances ownership rights (mubah in principle) with the prohibition of causing harm (la darar wa la dirar). Uncertainty triggers caution: the owner cannot build if harm is possible.
This is a talil (causative reasoning) based on the principle of preventing harm.
4
Apply the Mufta Bihi Rule
When issuing a fatwa, the operative ruling is: if it is certain that no harm will occur, the upper owner may build; otherwise, he may not. Certainty (yaqin) is required.
The tarjih authority is al-Walwaliji and later texts like al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة باب التحكيم
يجوز اتفاقاً . ولم يذكر المصنف منع صاحب العلو من التصرف في العلو لاختلاف المشايخ قال الولوالجي في كتاب القسمة علو لرجل وسفل لآخر اختلف المشايخ على قول أبي حنيفة قال بعضهم لصاحب العلو أن يبني ما بدا له ما لم يضر بالسفل، وذكر في بعض المواضع ليس له ذلك أضر بالسفل أو لم يضر هكذا ذكر في الجامع الصغير، والمختار للفتوى أنه إذا أشكل أنه يضر أم لا لا يملك وإذا علم أنه لا يضر يملك ا هـ. وجعله في الهداية على الخلاف السابق .
Book of Transfer (Hawalah), Chapter of Arbitration (Tahkim). It is permissible by agreement. And the author did not mention preventing the owner of the upper part from disposing in the upper part due to difference of the mashayikh. Al-Walwaliji said in the Book of Partition: An upper part belongs to a man and a lower part to another. The mashayikh differed on the statement of Abu Hanifa. Some said: The owner of the upper part may build whatever he wishes as long as it does not harm the lower part. And it is mentioned in some places that he does not have that right, whether it harms the lower part or not. This is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. And the chosen for fatwa is that if it is uncertain whether it harms or not, he does not have the right; if it is known that it does not harm, he has the right. And he placed it in al-Hidayah on the previous difference.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the rights of the owner of an upper floor (al-ulu) to make alterations or build in his property. The author states that it is permissible by agreement among the Hanafi scholars that some level of disposition is allowed. However, the author did not explicitly prohibit disposition because there is a difference among the mashayikh (later scholars) on the exact scope. Al-Walwaliji reports a scenario: a man owns the upper floor and another owns the lower floor. The mashayikh differ regarding the statement of Abu Hanifa. Some said the upper owner may build as he pleases as long as he does not cause harm (darar) to the lower owner. But in some other sources, it is reported that he does not have that right at all, whether it causes harm or not. The preferred position for fatwa (al-mukhtar li al-fatwa) is a compromise: if it is uncertain whether the construction causes harm, the upper owner does not have the right to build; if it is certain that it does not harm, he has the right. This ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifa's position, and al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah placed it under the earlier ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
علو (ulu): The upper floor or story of a building owned separately from the lower floor.
سفل (sufl): The lower floor or ground-level story of a building owned separately.
مختار للفتوى (al-mukhtar li al-fatwa): The preferred ruling adopted for issuing legal opinions in the Hanafi school, often chosen after weighing ikhtilaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred position for fatwa, when does the owner of the upper floor have the right to build in his upper portion?
A) Always, because he owns the property.
B) Only when the lower floor owner gives permission.
C) When it is certain that no harm will come to the lower floor.
D) When the lower floor owner is not present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi on building in an upper floor requires certainty that no harm will befall the lower floor owner.

Obligation to Rebuild a Demolished Support Wall
Ruling on lower owner demolishing support wall and distinction from collapse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the lower owner's obligation to rebuild the support wall and explain the distinction between intentional demolition and natural collapse.

Core Idea
Intentional demolition of a support wall triggers wajib rebuilding; natural collapse does not, because liability requires transgression.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common property dispute: what happens when the owner of a lower floor demolishes the wall that supports the upper floor? The Hanafi school unanimously prohibits such demolition, and if it occurs, the lower owner is forced to rebuild. However, if the wall collapses on its own, no such obligation arises. The key distinction lies in the presence of transgression (ta'addī). The text also explores related issues like ownership of ceiling components and plastering obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the lower owner demolishing the support wall (al-jidār al-ḥāmil). The ruling is that he is compelled (yujbar) to rebuild it. Determine the hukm level.
The hukm is wajib because it arises from ta'addi on another's right.
2
Examine the proof (dalil)
The text provides a qiyas (analogy) to two cases: a pawner who kills the pawned item, and a master who kills his slave who is in debt. Identify that this is qiyās jaliyy (clear analogy). The common effective cause ('illa) is transgression on another's property right.
The analogy is drawn from the principle that one who wrongfully destroys another's property must compensate.
3
Distinguish between demolition and collapse
Note the restriction: the ruling applies only when the lower owner himself demolishes (hadm). If the wall collapses naturally (inhidām) without his action, he is not forced to rebuild because there is no transgression.
This distinction is a fundamental legal principle: liability requires a wrongful act.
4
Apply the universal principle
From Fath al-Qadir: this is a universal principle (aṣl kullī): if someone is forced to have his partner do something and he does it without the partner's order, he is a volunteer (mutaṭawwi') because he could demand participation. But if the partner is not forced to act, then the one who acts is not a volunteer—he is compelled by necessity.
This principle refines the liability: only when the other party is under obligation does the acting party become a volunteer.
5
Note the subsidiary ruling on plastering
The passage concludes with a fatwa on plastering (taṭyīn): the upper owner is responsible for repairs but cannot be forced. This shows that obligations vary: some are enforceable (rebuilding the wall) and others are only religiously binding (ḥukman).
The distinction between compulsion (ijbār) and mere obligation (wujūb) is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اتفقوا على منع هدم صاحب السفل الجدار الحامل للعلو كما قدمناه فإن هدمه أجبر على بنائه لأنه تعدى على صاحب العلو بهدم ما هو قرار العلو كالراهن إذا قتل المرهون والمولى إذا قتل عبده المديون فرق بين حق التعلي وبين حق التسييل حيث لو هدم في الأوّل يجبر على البناء، ولو هدم في الثاني لا يجبر. وفي الذخيرة السفل إذا كان لرجل وعلو لآخر فسقف السفل وجذوعه وهراديه وبواريه وطينه لصاحب السفل غير أن صاحب العلو مسكنه في ذلك اهـ . وذكر الطرسوسي أن الهرادي ما يوضع فوق السقف إما من قصب أو من عريش. وذكر ابن وهبان أنه المكعب. وفي جامع الفصولين : لكل من صاحب السفل والعلو حق في ملك قوله : (فإن هدمه أجبر على بنائه الخ) قيد بهدمه له لأنه لو انهدم لا يجبر بدليل ما سيذكره قريباً من أنه لو انهدم السفل بغير صنع صاحبه لا يجبر على البناء لعدم التعدي الخ. وفي فتح القدير : وعلمت أنه ليس لصاحب السفل هدمه فلو هدمه يجبر على بنائه لأنه تعدى على صاحب العلو وهذا أصل كلي كل من أجبر على أن يفعل شريكه، فإذا فعل مع أحدهما بغير أمر شريكه فهو متطوع لأن له طريقاً وهو المطالبة بالمشاركة في الفعل كنهر بينهما امتنع أحدهما عن كريه وكري الآخر إلى آخر ما يأتي في آخر القولة الثانية . ثم قال : وإن كان لا يجبر لم يكن متطوعاً كعلو لرجل وسفل لآخر سقط السفل فبناه الآخر لا يكون متطوعاً لأنه لا يجبر صاحب السفل على بنائه فكان في بنائه إياه مضطراً ليصل إلى حقه الخ. فثبت الفرق بين الهدم والانهدام فتنبه قوله : (فسقف السفل وجذوعه وهراديه الخ) قال منلاعلي التركماني في مجموعته الفقهية وتطيينه لا يجب على واحد منهما، أما ذو العلو فلعدم وجوب إصلاح ملك الغير عليه، وأما ذو السفل فلعدم إجباره على إصلاح ملكه، وإن زال الطين عنه بتعدي الساكن وجب الضمان وإلا لا؛ كذا أفتى العلامة الخير الرملي رحمه الله تعالى كما هو مصرح في فتاويه في كتاب الدعوى مولانا حامد أفندي وفيها أيضاً وأجاب الشيخ اللطفي في فتاويه في مثل هذه المسألة بقوله سقف السفل لصاحب السفل غير أن لصاحب العلو حق السكني والمقام عليه ومرمة ذلك السقف من تطيين وغيره تلزمه غير أنه لا يجبر على ذلك والله سبحانه أعلم.
And they agreed on preventing the owner of the lower floor from demolishing the wall that supports the upper floor, as we have presented. If he demolishes it, he is forced to rebuild it because he transgressed against the owner of the upper floor by demolishing what is the foundation of the upper floor, like the pawner if he kills the pawned item, and the master if he kills his slave who is in debt. There is a distinction between a right of suspension (ta'liq) and a right of disposal (tasyil) where if one demolishes in the first case, he is forced to rebuild; if in the second, he is not forced. And in al-Dhakhira: If the lower floor belongs to a man and the upper to another, the ceiling of the lower, its beams, its crossbeams, its mats, and its mud belong to the owner of the lower floor, except that the owner of the upper floor has his dwelling therein. And al-Tarsusi mentioned that al-haradi is what is placed above the ceiling, either from reeds or from trellises. And Ibn Wahban mentioned that it is the cube. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: Each of the owners of the lower and upper has a right in the property. His statement: (If he demolishes it, he is forced to rebuild, etc.) He restricted it to his demolishing it because if it collapsed, he is not forced, as evidenced by what he will soon mention: that if the lower floor collapses without the action of its owner, he is not forced to rebuild due to lack of transgression. And in Fath al-Qadir: And I know that it is not permissible for the owner of the lower floor to demolish it, so if he demolishes it, he is forced to rebuild because he transgressed against the owner of the upper floor. This is a universal principle: everyone who is forced to have his partner do something, if one does it without the partner's order, he is a volunteer because he has a way (to demand participation). Like a river between them, one refuses to dredge it and the other dredges it, etc. Then he said: And if he was not forced, he would not be a volunteer, like an upper floor for a man and a lower for another, the lower collapsed and the other rebuilt it, he is not a volunteer because the owner of the lower is not forced to rebuild it, so in rebuilding it he was compelled to reach his right. Thus the difference between demolition and collapse is established, so take note. His statement: (Thus the ceiling of the lower, its beams, its crossbeams, etc.) Said Mulla 'Ali al-Turkumani in his Majmu'a al-Fiqhiyya: Its plastering is not obligatory on either one. As for the owner of the upper, due to the non-obligation of repairing another's property on him. As for the owner of the lower, due to not forcing him to repair his own property. And if the mud is removed by the dweller's transgression, then guarantee is obligatory; otherwise no. Thus issued the fatwa of 'Allama Khayr al-Ramli, as is clarified in his Fatawa in the Book of Claims, Mawlana Hamid Afandi. And in it also, Shaykh al-Lutfi responded in his Fatawa on such an issue by saying: The ceiling of the lower belongs to the owner of the lower, except that the owner of the upper has the right of dwelling and staying upon it, and the repair of that ceiling from plastering and other things is binding on him, but he is not forced to do it. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a key ruling regarding the support wall (al-jidar al-hamil) between the lower and upper floors. The Hanafi school unanimously prohibits the lower owner from demolishing this wall because it supports the upper floor. If he demolishes it, he is compelled (yujbar) to rebuild it because he has transgressed (ta'adda) against the upper owner by removing the foundation of his property. The text draws an analogy: just as a pawner who kills the pawned item is liable, the lower owner here is liable to restore the wall. However, if the wall collapses on its own (inhidam) without the owner's action, he is not forced to rebuild because there is no transgression. The passage then shifts to ownership of the ceiling components: the lower owner owns the ceiling, beams, and mud, but the upper owner has the right to dwell upon it. Later, it addresses plastering (tatyin): neither owner is obligated to plaster, unless the occupant (sakin) causes the mud to fall, in which case the occupant is liable. The preferred fatwa is that repair of the ceiling is binding on the upper owner, but he cannot be forced to do it. This detailed discussion illustrates the Hanafi approach to balancing property rights with obligations.

Key Terms
هدم (hadm): Intentional demolition by the owner's action, triggering liability.
انهدام (inhidam): Collapse without human action, not triggering liability to rebuild.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression or wrongful act that causes harm to another's property right.
جدار حامل (jidaar haamil): The wall that supports the upper floor; lower owner cannot demolish it.
هرادي (haradi): The crossbeams or trellis placed above the ceiling for support.
بواريه (bawari): Mats (often made of reeds) used in the ceiling structure.
تطيين (tatyin): Plastering or mudding the ceiling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the lower owner intentionally demolishes the support wall, what is the ruling?
A) He is not forced to rebuild because he owns the wall.
B) He is forced to rebuild because it is a transgression on the upper owner's right.
C) He must only pay compensation, not rebuild.
D) The upper owner must rebuild at his own cost.

Key Takeaway
Intentional demolition of a shared support wall creates a wajib obligation to rebuild, while natural collapse does not, due to the absence of transgression.

Opening Doors in Branching Dead-End Alleys: The Hanafi Ruling and Dispute
Commentary on the ruling prohibiting door openings in branching dead-end alleys and the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on opening a door in a branching dead-end alley and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over its permissibility.

Core Idea
In dead-end alleys, property rights limit passage; opening a new door risks prescriptive claims, hence prohibition.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles a common urban scenario: an owner in a primary dead-end alley wants to open a door into a secondary dead-end alley that branches off the first. While some Hanafi scholars permit this as a simple exercise of property rights, the relied-upon view prevents it to head off future disputes over right of passage. The text also contrasts this with circular alleys where all owners share passage rights, and explores edge cases like opening a second door higher up on one’s own wall.

Guided Path
1
Identify the physical layout and the legal question
Picture an elongated dead-end alley (zā'igha mustatīla) from which another dead-end alley branches off. The legal question is: may an owner of a house in the first alley open a door into the second alley?
The second alley must also be non-through (ghayr nāfidha) for the prohibition.
2
State the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) ruling
The relied-upon opinion, attributed to Imam Muhammad in al-Jami', is that it is prohibited (haram) to open such a door. The reason: opening implies a right of passage, but the owner has no right to pass through the second alley since it is not his property.
This is the view adopted by most later Hanafi authorities.
3
Analyze the opposing opinion and its reasoning
Some scholars, like Shaykh al-Islam, allowed it absolutely. Their argument: the owner could remove his entire wall, so opening a door is a lesser action; any potential passage can be prevented by agreement. However, the majority responded that after opening, long-term use could lead to a prescriptive right, and monitoring its enforcement is impractical.
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf reflects different weight given to ownership rights vs. community stability.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and underlying rationale
The fatwa upholds the prohibition, as recorded in al-Fatawa al-Ghiyathiyya by Sadr al-Shahid. The rationale is to block the means (sadd al-dharā’i‘) to future disputes. The exception is a circular alley (mustadīra), where all owners share the entire space as a common courtyard, so opening a door is permissible.
This ruling also applies when a house is sold: only owners in the same alley have pre-emption rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيها باباً بخلاف المستديرة ادعى داراً في يد وقت البناء وهو مبني على أن المبني يبني على ملك الشريك أو على ملك الباني ثم ينتقل منه أيضاً. وفي جامع الفصولين جدار بينهما ولكل منهما حموله فوهى الحائط فأراد أحدهما رفعه ليصلحه وأبى الآخر ينبغي أن يقول مريد الإصلاح للآخر ارفع حمولتك بأسطوانات وعمد ويعلمه أنه يريد رفعه في وقت كذا وأشهد على ذلك فلو فعله وإلا فله رفع الجدار فلو سقط حمولته لم يضمن ا هـ . قوله : (زائغة مستطيلة يتشعب عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيها باباً بخلاف المستديرة أي سكة كما في المعراج، وفسرها تاج الشريعة بالسكة غير النافذة سميت بذلك لزيغها عن الطريق الأعظم، وفسرها في غاية البيان بالمحلة سميت بها لميلها من طرف إلى طرف من زاغت الشمس إذا مالت وفي التهذيب الزائغة الطريق الذي حاد عن الأعظم، والمستطيلة الطويلة من استصال بمعنى طال . ولم يقيد المؤلف الأولى صريحاً بكونها غير نافذة تبعاً لما في أكثر الكتب وقيدها في الهداية تبعاً للفقيه أبي الليث والتمرتاشي، ويمكن أن يفهم كلام المؤلف عليه لقوله مثلها غير نافذة فجعل الثانية كالأولى بقيد عدم

(۱) هكذا الرسم بالأصل ولينظر فيه فإنه عين الأولى وليست مستديرة اهـ مصحح.
Book of Hawalah, Chapter of Tahkim. ... Like it, non-through, the dwellers of the first may not open a door in it, unlike the circular. ... And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: A wall between them, each having a load, the wall weakened, one wants to raise it to repair, the other refuses; he should say to the other: 'Remove your load with pillars and posts' and notify that he intends to raise it at such time and take witnesses. If he does it, else he may raise the wall; if his load falls, he is not liable. End. His saying: 'A zigzag elongated alley from which branches another like it, non-through; the dwellers of the first may not open a door therein, unlike the circular' i.e., a dead-end alley as in al-Mi'raj. Taj al-Shari'ah interpreted it as a non-through alley, named for its deviation from the main road. ... And the author did not explicitly restrict the first as non-through, following most books; but al-Hidayah restricted it following Abu al-Layth and al-Tamirtashi. ... The picture of the elongated alley is thus: O the road O O O O O O. So the one who can open a door in the furthest zigzag is the owner of the house at the corner of the second zigzag. We said he does not have that because his opening is for passage, and the dwellers of the first zigzag have no right of passage in the second. ... Al-Ramli said: Apparently the ruling is the same, no consideration whether the first is through or not, because its dwellers are absolutely prevented from passing in the second. So the author unconditionally included through and non-through. He restricted the branching alley to non-through because if it were through, the public could pass, so opening a door for the first dwellers would not be prevented. ... The meaning of 'unlike the circular' is that if the branching alley were circular, they may open because each inhabitant has right of passage in all of it as it is a shared courtyard; only it has curvature, so all share in pre-emption if a house there is sold. Its picture is: O O O O 10 : Know. In al-Tatarakhaniyya from al-Fatawa al-Ghiyathiyya from al-Sadr al-Shahid Husam al-Din that the fatwa is on prohibition; so it is established that there is difference in the matter, so refer to the apparent narration. Consider: A man owns a house in a non-through alley with a door; he wants to open another door higher up; he may do so, mentioned by Qadikhan. I say: Qadikhan's absolute statement allows it even if he does not close the first. I saw in Shafi'i books that he must close it; similar might apply in our school. Qadikhan mentioned in al-Shurb: If one with a right of way in a non-through alley wants to make his door at the lower end, there is disagreement; some say not allowed, but in the Book it is allowed; the two cases are equated, and the Imams al-Sarakhsi held that. I say: The apparent disagreement here is based on difference in narration; the apparent narration is prohibition. Al-Zayla'i mentioned: It is feared that he will close his original door and suffice with the new one, making his house from that alley. This implies no obligation to close the first door, otherwise he would have spoken of obligation. His saying: 'Unlike the dwellers of the furthest' requires by reasoning that this applies when the house in the furthest is at the corner of the first elongated alley on the side of crossing; if at the second corner, he has no right of passage.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) on a passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq concerning the rights of owners in dead-end alleys (sikka ghayr nāfidha) and their branches. The author, al-Bahr al-Raiq, expands on the ruling that the dwellers of the first (primary) alley cannot open a door into a secondary alley that branches off from it, when both are non-through. The commentary clarifies that the primary alley need not be explicitly non-through as per the matn; the restriction effectively arises from the second being non-through. The key distinction: if the secondary alley is circular (mustadīra), all owners share passage rights and may open doors. The text then delves into intra-madhhab differences: some authorities allow the owner of a house at the corner of the secondary alley to open a door, but the relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwayah) prohibits it to prevent false claims of right of passage. There is also discussion on whether one can open an additional door higher up in the alley—Qadikhan permits it absolutely, but the commentator notes a Shafi'i view requiring closure of the first door, suggesting a potential similar ruling in the Hanafi school to avoid nuisance. The passage is a prime example of Hanafi property law reasoning, balancing individual ownership with communal rights.

Key Terms
زائغة (zā'igha): A winding or zigzag alley that deviates from the main road; often a dead-end alley
مستطيلة (mustatīla): Elongated; describes the shape of the primary alley
متشعبة (mutasha''iba): Branching alley that splits off from the first
غير نافذة (ghayr nāfidha): Dead-end, not opening to a public thoroughfare
مستديرة (mustadīra): Circular alley where all inhabitants share the entire space as a courtyard

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dead-end alley (zā'igha) with a branching non-through alley, can the owner of a house in the first alley open a door into the second alley according to the relied-upon Hanafi view?
A) Yes, because he owns his wall.
B) No, because he has no right of passage there.
C) Yes, only if he does not pass through.
D) No, unless the second alley is circular.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon view prohibits opening a door from a primary dead-end alley into a branching dead-end alley to prevent future claims of prescriptive rights, with an exception for circular alleys.

Opening a Door in a Branched Alley: Right of Passage and Prohibition
Ruling on opening a door in a branched dead-end alley based on right of passage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on opening a door in a branched dead-end alley and distinguish it from a shared curved alley.

Core Idea
Opening a door in a dead-end alley where you have no passage right is prohibited, because prevention after opening is impracticable.

In Hanafi law, dead-end alleys are treated as shared property among their inhabitants. When a dead-end alley branches into another, questions arise about who may open new doors. This passage clarifies the rules based on rights of passage, and addresses a subtle intra-madhhab dispute about whether the prohibition applies to the door itself or only to its use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alley types
Distinguish between the main dead-end alley (الأولى), the branched dead-end alley (القصوى), a through alley (النافذة), and a curved shared courtyard (المستديرة). Each has different passage rights.
2
Determine right of passage
Inhabitans of the branched alley have passage right in the main alley; the reverse is not true. Inhabitants of a curved shared alley all have equal passage rights.
3
Apply the ruling on opening a door
Only those with a right of passage may open a door into the alley. Opening without such right is prohibited, and the prohibition is on the act of opening itself, not just on using the door.
The text mentions a dispute: some say prohibition is on use only, but the correct view (al-aṣahḥ) is prohibition of opening, as stated by Muhammad al-Shaybānī in al-Jāmi'.
4
Understand the rationale
Prevention of use after opening is impracticable because monitoring is difficult and the owner could later claim a right of passage based on the door's existence.
5
Compare with the curved alley
In a curved alley that is a shared courtyard, all inhabitants have full passage rights, so opening a door is permissible, and all share in pre-emption rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزائغة القصوى بل هو لأهلها على الخصوص ولذا لو بيعت دار في القصوى لم يكن لأهل الأولى شفعة بخلاف أهل القصوى فإن لأحدهم أن يفتح باباً في الأولى لأن له حق المرور فيها، وبخلاف النافذة فإن المرور فيها حق العامة، ولا خلاف أن له أن يفتح . وقال البعض : إنه لا يمنع من الفتح بل من المرور لأن فتح الباب رفع جداره وله رفعه كله فله رفع بعضه والأصح المنع من الفتح نص عليه محمد في الجامع، ولأن المنع بعد الفتح لا يمكن لعسر المراقبة وربما على طول الزمان يدعي حق المرور مستدلاً بفتح الباب ويكون القول له للظاهر معه وهو فتح الباب. وقوله بخلاف المستديرة معناه لو كانت المتشبعة مستديرة فلهم أن يفتحوا لأن لكل متهم حق المرور في كلها إذ هي ساحة مشتركة. غاية الأمر أن فيها اعوجاجاً ولذا الكل يشتركون في الشفعة إذا بيعت دار فيها وهذه صورتها (۱).

The (alley called) 'the farthest curve' belongs specifically to its inhabitants. Therefore, if a house in the farthest curve is sold, the inhabitants of the first (alley) have no pre-emption, unlike the inhabitants of the farthest curve, because one of them can open a door in the first (alley) due to having right of passage in it. Unlike a through alley, where passage is a public right. There is no disagreement that he may open (a door). Some said: He is not prevented from opening (the door) but from (using) passage, because opening a door is removing his wall, and he may remove it entirely, so he may remove part of it. The correct view is prevention of opening, as stated by Muhammad in al-Jami'. Prevention after opening is impossible because monitoring is difficult, and over time he might claim right of passage based on the open door, and his statement would be accepted due to the apparent evidence which is the open door. His statement: 'unlike the curved alley' means if the branched alley is curved, they may open (doors) because each inhabitant has right of passage in its entirety since it is a shared courtyard. The only thing is that it has a curve, and therefore all share in pre-emption when a house in it is sold. Here is its diagram.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage discusses the right to open a new door in a dead-end alley that is a branch (متشعب) of a longer dead-end alley. The author explains that the inhabitants of the main alley (الأولى) have no right to open a door into the branched alley (القصوى) because they do not have a right of passage in it. Conversely, inhabitants of the branched alley can open a door into the main alley because they do have passage rights. The passage also contrasts this with a through alley where passage is a public right. There is a subsidiary dispute: some scholars say the prohibition is on using the door for passage, not on opening it, but the correct view (text from Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Jami') is that the opening itself is prohibited, because preventing use after opening is impractical. For curved alleys that are shared courtyards, all inhabitants share passage rights, so opening is allowed and all share in pre-emption rights.

Key Terms
الزائغة (al-zā'igha): A dead-end alley that branches off from a main dead-end alley.
المتشعبة (al-mutasha''iba): The branched alley (synonym for الزائغة).
السكة غير النافذة (al-sikkah ghayr al-nāfidhah): A dead-end alley; a lane that does not lead to a public thoroughfare.
المستديرة (al-mustadīrah): A curved alley that is treated as a shared courtyard (ساحة مشتركة) due to its shape and common use.
الأولى (al-ūlā): The main dead-end alley from which the branched alley extends.
القصوى (al-quṣwā): The farthest or branched alley (synonym for الزائغة).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because he has no right of passage in the branched alley.
C) Because the branched alley is a public road.
D) Because opening a door is always prohibited in dead-end alleys.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, opening a door in a dead-end alley requires a right of passage; without it, even the opening is prohibited to prevent future disputes.
